apparently well kept; though the odd thing was that it seemed to begin
there.</content>
<content>We walked down and stood on it, but one single hundred paces
behind us, in the direction of Sheba's Breasts, it vanished, the entire
surface of the mountain being strewn with boulders interspersed with
patches of snow.</content>
<content>"What do you make of this, Quatermain?"</content>
<content>asked Sir Henry.</content>
<content>I shook my head, I could make nothing of the thing.</content>
<content>"I have it!"</content>
<content>said Good; "the road no doubt ran right over the range and
across the desert on the other side, but the sand there has covered it
up, and above us it has been obliterated by some volcanic eruption of
molten lava."</content>
<content>This seemed a good suggestion; at any rate, we accepted it, and
proceeded down the mountain.</content>
<content>It proved a very different business
travelling along down hill on that magnificent pathway with full
stomachs from what it was travelling uphill over the snow quite starved
and almost frozen.</content>
<content>Indeed, had it not been for melancholy recollections
of poor Ventv"gel's sad fate, and of that grim cave where he kept
company with the old Dom, we should have felt positively cheerful,
notwithstanding the sense of unknown dangers before us.</content>
<content>Every mile we
walked the atmosphere grew softer and balmier, and the country before
us shone with a yet more luminous beauty.</content>
<content>As for the road itself, I
never saw such an engineering work, though Sir Henry said that the
great road over the St. Gothard in Switzerland is very similar.</content>
<content>No
difficulty had been too great for the Old World engineer who laid it
out.</content>
<content>At one place we came to a ravine three hundred feet broad and at
least a hundred feet deep.</content>
<content>This vast gulf was actually filled in with
huge blocks of dressed stone, having arches pierced through them at the
bottom for a waterway, over which the road went on sublimely.</content>
<content>At
another place it was cut in zigzags out of the side of a precipice five
hundred feet deep, and in a third it tunnelled through the base of an
intervening ridge, a space of thirty yards or more.</content>
<content>Here we noticed that the sides of the tunnel were covered with quaint
sculptures, mostly of mailed figures driving in chariots.</content>
<content>One, which
was exceedingly beautiful, represented a whole battle scene with a
convoy of captives being marched off in the distance.</content>
<content>"Well," said Sir Henry, after inspecting this ancient work of art, "it
is very well to call this Solomon's Road, but my humble opinion is that
the Egyptians had been here before Solomon's people ever set a foot on
it.</content>
<content>If this isn't  Egyptian or Phoenician handiwork, I must say that it
is very like it."</content>
<content>By midday we had advanced sufficiently down the mountain to search the
region where wood was to be met with.</content>
<content>First we came to scattered bushes
which grew more and more frequent, till at last we found the road
winding through a vast grove of silver trees similar to those which are
to be seen on the slopes of Table Mountain at Cape Town.</content>
<content>I had never
before met with them in all my wanderings, except at the Cape, and
their appearance here astonished me greatly.</content>
<content>"Ah!"</content>
<content>said Good, surveying these shining-leaved trees with evident
enthusiasm, "here is lots of wood, let us stop and cook some dinner; I
have about digested that raw heart."</content>
<content>Nobody objected to this, so leaving the road we made our way to a
stream which was babbling away not far off, and soon had a goodly fire
of dry boughs blazing.</content>
<content>Cutting off some substantial hunks from the
flesh of the inco which we had brought with us, we proceeded to toast
them on the end of sharp sticks, as one sees the Kafirs do, and ate
them with relish.</content>
<content>After filling ourselves, we lit our pipes and gave
ourselves up to enjoyment that, compared with the hardships we had
recently undergone, seemed almost heavenly.</content>
<content>The brook, of which the banks were clothed with dense masses of a
gigantic species of maidenhair fern interspersed with feathery tufts of
wild asparagus, sung merrily at our side, the soft air murmured through
the leaves of the silver trees, doves cooed around, and bright-winged
birds flashed like living gems from bough to bough.</content>
<content>It was a Paradise.</content>
<content>The magic of the place combined with an overwhelming sense of dangers
left behind, and of the promised land reached at last, seemed to charm
us into silence.</content>
<content>Sir Henry and Umbopa sat conversing in a mixture of
broken English and Kitchen Zulu in a low voice, but earnestly enough,
and I lay, with my eyes half shut, upon that fragrant bed of fern and
watched them.</content>
<content>Presently I missed Good, and I looked to see what had become of him.</content>
<content>Soon I observed him sitting by the bank of the stream, in which he had
been bathing.</content>
<content>He had nothing on but his flannel shirt, and his natural
habits of extreme neatness having reasserted themselves, he was
actively employed in making a most elaborate toilet.</content>
<content>He had washed his
gutta-percha collar, had thoroughly shaken out his trousers, coat and
waistcoat, and was now folding them up neatly till he was ready to put
them on, shaking his head sadly as he scanned the numerous rents and
tears in them, which naturally had resulted from our frightful journey.</content>
<content>Then he took his boots, scrubbed them with a handful of fern, and
finally rubbed them over with a piece of fat, which he had carefully
saved from the inco meat, till they looked, comparatively speaking,
respectable.</content>
<content>Having inspected them judiciously through his eye-glass,
he put the boots on and began a fresh operation.</content>
<content>From a little bag that
he carried he produced a pocket-comb in which was fixed a tiny
looking-glass, and in this he surveyed himself.</content>
<content>Apparently he was not
satisfied, for he proceeded to do his hair with great care.</content>
<content>Then came a
pause whilst he again contemplated the effect; still it was not
satisfactory.</content>
<content>He felt his chin, on which the accumulated scrub of a ten
days' beard was flourishing.</content>
<content>"Surely," thought I, "he is not going to try to shave."</content>
<content>But so it was.</content>
<content>Taking the piece of fat with which he had greased his boots, Good
washed it thoroughly in the stream.</content>
<content>Then diving again into the bag he
brought out a little pocket razor with a guard to it, such as are
bought by people who are afraid of cutting themselves, or by those
about to undertake a sea voyage.</content>
<content>Then he rubbed his face and chin
vigorously with the fat and began.</content>
<content>Evidently it proved a painful
process, for he groaned very much over it, and I was convulsed with
inward laughter as I watched him struggling with that stubbly beard.</content>
<content>It
seemed so very odd that a man should take the trouble to shave himself
with a piece of fat in such a place and in our circumstances.</content>
<content>At last
he succeeded in getting the hair off the right side of his face and
chin, when suddenly I, who was watching, became conscious of a flash of
light that passed just by his head.</content>
<content>Good sprang up with a profane exclamation  if it had not been a safety
razor he would certainly have cut his throat , and so did I, without
the exclamation, and this was what I saw.</content>
<content>Standing not more than twenty
paces from where I was, and ten from Good, were a group of men.</content>
<content>They
were very tall and copper-coloured, and some of them wore great plumes
of black feathers and short cloaks of leopard skins; this was all I
noticed at the moment.</content>
<content>In front of them stood a youth of about
seventeen, his hand still raised and his body bent forward in the
attitude of a Grecian statue of a spear-thrower.</content>
<content>Evidently the flash of
light had been caused by a weapon which he had hurled.</content>
<content>As I looked an old soldier-like man stepped forward out of the group,
and catching the youth by the arm said something to him.</content>
<content>Then they
advanced upon us.</content>
<content>Sir Henry, Good, and Umbopa by this time had seized their rifles and
lifted them threateningly.</content>
<content>The party of natives still came on.</content>
<content>It
struck me that they could not know what rifles were, or they would not
have treated them with such contempt.</content>
<content>"Put down your guns!"</content>
<content>I halloed to the others, seeing that our only
chance of safety lay in conciliation.</content>
<content>They obeyed, and walking to the
front I addressed the elderly man who had checked the youth.</content>
<content>"Greeting," I said in Zulu, not knowing what language to use.</content>
<content>To my
surprise I was understood.</content>
<content>"Greeting," answered the old man, not, indeed, in the same tongue, but
in a dialect so closely allied to it that neither Umbopa nor myself had
any difficulty in understanding him.</content>
<content>Indeed, as we afterwards found
out, the language spoken by this people is an old-fashioned form of the
Zulu tongue, bearing about the same relationship to it that the English
of Chaucer does to the English of the nineteenth century.</content>
<content>"Whence come you?"</content>
<content>he went on, "who are you?</content>
<content>and why are the faces of
three of you white, and the face of the fourth as the face of our
mother's sons?"</content>
<content>and he pointed to Umbopa.</content>
<content>I looked at Umbopa as he said
it, and it flashed across me that he was right.</content>
<content>The face of Umbopa was
like the faces of the men before me, and so was his great form like
their forms.</content>
<content>But I had not time to reflect on this coincidence.</content>
<content>"We are strangers, and come in peace," I answered, speaking very
slowly, so that he might understand me, "and this man is our servant."</content>
<content>"You lie," he answered; "no strangers can cross the mountains where all
things perish.</content>
<content>But what do your lies matter?</content>
<content>- if ye are strangers then
ye must die, for no strangers may live in the land of the Kukuanas.</content>
<content>It
is the king's law.</content>
<content>Prepare then to die, O strangers!"</content>
<content>I was slightly staggered at this, more especially as I saw the hands of
some of the men steal down to their sides, where hung on each what
looked to me like a large and heavy knife.</content>
<content>"What does that beggar say?"</content>
<content>asked Good.</content>
<content>"He says we are going to be killed," I answered grimly.</content>
<content>"Oh, Lord!"</content>
<content>groaned Good; and, as was his way when perplexed, he put
his hand to his false teeth, dragging the top set down and allowing
them to fly back to his jaw with a snap.</content>
<content>It was a most fortunate move,
for next second the dignified crowd of Kukuanas uttered a simultaneous
yell of horror, and bolted back some yards.</content>
<content>"What's up?"</content>
<content>said I.</content>
<content>"It's his teeth," whispered Sir Henry excitedly.</content>
<content>"He moved them.</content>
<content>Take
them out, Good, take them out!"</content>
<content>He obeyed, slipping the set into the sleeve of his flannel shirt.</content>
<content>In another second curiosity had overcome fear, and the men advanced
slowly.</content>
<content>Apparently they had now forgotten their amiable intention of
killing us.</content>
<content>"How is it, O strangers," asked the old man solemnly, "that this fat
man  pointing to Good, who was clad in nothing but boots and a flannel
shirt, and had only half finished his shaving , whose body is clothed,
and whose legs are bare, who grows hair on one side of his sickly face
and not on the other, and who wears one shining and transparent
eye - how is it, I ask, that he has teeth which move of themselves,
coming away from the jaws and returning of their own will?"</content>
<content>"Open your mouth," I said to Good, who promptly curled up his lips and
grinned at the old gentleman like an angry dog, revealing to his
astonished gaze two thin red lines of gum as utterly innocent of
ivories as a new-born elephant.</content>
<content>The audience gasped.</content>
<content>"Where are his teeth?"</content>
<content>they shouted; "with our eyes we saw them."</content>
<content>Turning his head slowly and with a gesture of ineffable contempt, Good
swept his hand across his mouth.</content>
<content>Then he grinned again, and lo, there
were two rows of lovely teeth.</content>
<content>Now the young man who had flung the knife threw himself down on the
grass and gave vent to a prolonged howl of terror; and as for the old
gentleman, his knees knocked together with fear.</content>
<content>"I see that ye are spirits," he said falteringly; "did ever man born of
woman have hair on one side of his face and not on the other, or a
round and transparent eye, or teeth which moved and melted away and
grew again?</content>
<content>Pardon us, O my lords."</content>
<content>Here was luck indeed, and, needless to say, I jumped at the chance.</content>
<content>"It is granted," I said with an imperial smile.</content>
<content>"Nay, ye shall know the
truth.</content>
<content>We come from another world, though we are men such as ye; we
come," I went on, "from the biggest star that shines at night."</content>
<content>"Oh!</content>
<content>oh!"</content>
<content>groaned the chorus of astonished aborigines.</content>
<content>"Yes," I went on, "we do, indeed"; and again I smiled benignly, as I
uttered that amazing lie.</content>
<content>"We come to stay with you a little while, and
to bless you by our sojourn.</content>
<content>Ye will see, O friends, that I have
prepared myself for this visit by the learning of your language."</content>
<content>"It is so, it is so," said the chorus.</content>
<content>"Only, my lord," put in the old gentleman, "thou hast learnt it very
badly."</content>
<content>I cast an indignant glance at him, and he quailed.</content>
<content>"Now friends," I continued, "ye might think that after so long a
journey we should find it in our hearts to avenge such a reception,
mayhap to strike cold in death the imperious hand that - that, in
short - threw a knife at the head of him whose teeth come and go."</content>
<content>"Spare him, my lords," said the old man in supplication; "he is the
king's son, and I am his uncle.</content>
<content>If anything befalls him his blood will
be required at my hands."</content>
<content>"Yes, that is certainly so," put in the young man with great emphasis.</content>
<content>"Ye may perhaps doubt our power to avenge," I went on, heedless of this
by-play.</content>
<content>"Stay, I will show you.</content>
<content>Here, thou dog and slave  addressing
Umbopa in a savage tone , give me the magic tube that speaks"; and I
tipped a wink towards my express rifle.</content>
<content>Umbopa rose to the occasion, and with something as nearly resembling a
grin as I have ever seen on his dignified face he handed me the gun.</content>
<content>"It is here, O Lord of Lords," he said with a deep obeisance.</content>
<content>Now just before I had asked for the rifle I had perceived a little
 klipspringer antelope standing on a mass of rock about seventy yards
away, and determined to risk the shot.</content>
<content>"Ye see that buck," I said, pointing the animal out to the party before
me.</content>
<content>"Tell me, is it possible for man born of woman to kill it from here
with a noise?"</content>
<content>"It is not possible, my lord," answered the old man.</content>
<content>"Yet shall I kill it," I said quietly.</content>
<content>The old man smiled.</content>
<content>"That my lord cannot do," he answered.</content>
<content>I raised the rifle and covered the buck.</content>
<content>It was a small animal, and one
which a man might well be excused for missing, but I knew that it would
not do to miss.</content>
<content>I drew a deep breath, and slowly pressed on the trigger.</content>
<content>The buck stood
still as a stone.</content>
<content>"Bang!</content>
<content>thud!"</content>
<content>The antelope sprang into the air and fell on the rock
dead as a door nail.</content>
<content>A groan of simultaneous terror burst from the group before us.</content>
<content>"If you want meat," I remarked coolly, "go fetch that buck."</content>
<content>The old man made a sign, and one of his followers departed, and
presently returned bearing the klipspringer .</content>
<content>I noticed with
satisfaction that I had hit it fairly behind the shoulder.</content>
<content>They
gathered round the poor creature's body, gazing at the bullet-hole in
consternation.</content>
<content>"Ye see," I said, "I do not speak empty words."</content>
<content>There was no answer.</content>
<content>"If ye yet doubt our power," I went on, "let one of you go stand upon
that rock that I may make him as this buck."</content>
<content>None of them seemed at all inclined to take the hint, till at last the
king's son spoke.</content>
<content>"It is well said.</content>
<content>Do thou, my uncle, go stand upon the rock.</content>
<content>It is but
a buck that the magic has killed.</content>
<content>Surely it cannot kill a man."</content>
<content>The old gentleman did not take the suggestion in good part.</content>
<content>Indeed, he
seemed hurt.</content>
<content>"No!</content>
<content>no!"</content>
<content>he ejaculated hastily, "my old eyes have seen enough.</content>
<content>These
are wizards, indeed.</content>
<content>Let us bring them to the king.</content>
<content>Yet if any should
wish a further proof, let him stand upon the rock, that the magic
tube may speak with him."</content>
<content>There was a most general and hasty expression of dissent.</content>
<content>"Let not good magic be wasted on our poor bodies," said one; "we are
satisfied.</content>
<content>All the witchcraft of our people cannot show the like of
this."</content>
<content>"It is so," remarked the old gentleman, in a tone of intense relief;
"without any doubt it is so.</content>
<content>Listen, children of the Stars, children of
the shining Eye and the movable Teeth, who roar out in thunder, and
slay from afar.</content>
<content>I am Infadoos, son of Kafa, once king of the Kukuana
people.</content>
<content>This youth is Scragga."</content>
<content>"He nearly scragged me," murmured Good.</content>
<content>"Scragga, son of Twala, the great king - Twala, husband of a thousand
wives, chief and lord paramount of the Kukuanas, keeper of the great
Road, terror of his enemies, student of the Black Arts, leader of a
hundred thousand warriors, Twala the One-eyed, the Black, the Terrible."</content>
<content>"So," said I superciliously, "lead us then to Twala.</content>
<content>We do not talk
with low people and underlings."</content>
<content>"It is well, my lords, we will lead you; but the way is long.</content>
<content>We are
hunting three days' journey from the place of the king.</content>
<content>But let my
lords have patience, and we will lead them."</content>
<content>"So be it," I said carelessly; "all time is before us, for we do not
die.</content>
<content>We are ready, lead on.</content>
<content>But Infadoos, and thou Scragga, beware!</content>
<content>Play us no monkey tricks, set for us no foxes' snares, for before your
brains of mud have thought of them we shall know and avenge.</content>
<content>The light
of the transparent eye of him with the bare legs and the half-haired
face shall destroy you, and go through your land; his vanishing teeth
shall affix themselves fast in you and eat you up, you and your wives
and children; the magic tubes shall argue with you loudly, and make you
as sieves.</content>
<content>Beware!"</content>
<content>This magnificent address did not fail of its effect; indeed, it might
almost have been spared, so deeply were our friends already impressed
with our powers.</content>
<content>The old man made a deep obeisance, and murmured the words, " Koom
Koom ," which I afterwards discovered was their royal salute,
corresponding to the BayÃ©te of the Zulus, and turning, addressed his
followers.</content>
<content>These at once proceeded to lay hold of all our goods and
chattels, in order to bear them for us, excepting only the guns, which
they would on no account touch.</content>
<content>They even seized Good's clothes, that,
as the reader may remember, were neatly folded up beside him.</content>
<content>He saw and made a dive for them, and a loud altercation ensued.</content>
<content>"Let not my lord of the transparent Eye and the melting Teeth touch
them," said the old man.</content>
<content>"Surely his slave shall carry the things."</content>
<content>"But I want to put 'em on!"</content>
<content>roared Good, in nervous English.</content>
<content>Umbopa translated.</content>
<content>"Nay, my lord," answered Infadoos, "would my lord cover up his
beautiful white legs  although he is so dark Good has a singularly
white skin  from the eyes of his servants?</content>
<content>Have we offended my lord
that he should do such a thing?"</content>
<content>Here I nearly exploded with laughing; and meanwhile one of the men
started on with the garments.</content>
<content>"Damn it!"</content>
<content>roared Good, "that black villain has got my trousers."</content>
<content>"Look here, Good," said Sir Henry; "you have appeared in this country
in a certain character, and you must live up to it.</content>
<content>It will never do
for you to put on trousers again.</content>
<content>Henceforth you must exist in a
flannel shirt, a pair of boots, and an eye-glass."</content>
<content>"Yes," I said, "and with whiskers on one side of your face and not on
the other.</content>
<content>If you change any of these things the people will think that
we are impostors.</content>
<content>I am very sorry for you, but, seriously, you must.</content>
<content>If
once they begin to suspect us our lives will not be worth a brass
farthing."</content>
<content>"Do you really think so?"</content>
<content>said Good gloomily.</content>
<content>"I do, indeed.</content>
<content>Your 'beautiful white legs' and your eye-glass are now
 the features of our party, and as Sir Henry says, you must live up to
them.</content>
<content>Be thankful that you have got your boots on, and that the air is
warm."</content>
<content>Good sighed, and said no more, but it took him a fortnight to become
accustomed to his new and scant attire.</content>
<content>CHAPTER VIII

WE ENTER KUKUANALAND

All that afternoon we travelled along the magnificent roadway, which
trended steadily in a north-westerly direction.</content>
<content>Infadoos and Scragga
walked with us, but their followers marched about one hundred paces
ahead.</content>
<content>"Infadoos," I said at length, "who made this road?"</content>
<content>"It was made, my lord, of old time, none know how or when, not even the
wise woman Gagool, who has lived for generations.</content>
<content>We are not old enough
to remember its making.</content>
<content>None can fashion such roads now, but the king
suffers no grass to grow upon it."</content>
<content>"And whose are the writings on the wall of the caves through which we
have passed on the road?"</content>
<content>I asked, referring to the Egyptian-like
sculptures that we had seen.</content>
<content>"My lord, the hands that made the road wrote the wonderful writings.</content>
<content>We
know not who wrote them."</content>
<content>"When did the Kukuana people come into this country?"</content>
<content>"My lord, the race came down here like the breath of a storm ten
thousand thousand moons ago, from the great lands which lie there
beyond," and he pointed to the north.</content>
<content>"They could travel no further
because of the high mountains which ring in the land, so say the old
voices of our fathers that have descended to us the children, and so
says Gagool, the wise woman, the smeller out of witches," and again he
pointed to the snow-clad peaks.</content>
<content>"The country, too, was good, so they
settled here and grew strong and powerful, and now our numbers are like
the sea sand, and when Twala the king calls up his regiments their
plumes cover the plain so far as the eye of man can reach."</content>
<content>"And if the land is walled in with mountains, who is there for the
regiments to fight with?"</content>
<content>"Nay, my lord, the country is open there towards the north, and now and
again warriors sweep down upon us in clouds from a land we know not,
and we slay them.</content>
<content>It is the third part of the life of a man since there
was a war.</content>
<content>Many thousands died in it, but we destroyed those who came
to eat us up.</content>
<content>So since then there has been no war."</content>
<content>"Your warriors must grow weary of resting on their spears, Infadoos."</content>
<content>"My lord, there was one war, just after we destroyed the people that
came down upon us, but it was a civil war; dog ate dog."</content>
<content>"How was that?"</content>
<content>"My lord the king, my half-brother, had a brother born at the same
birth, and of the same woman.</content>
<content>It is not our custom, my lord, to suffer
twins to live; the weaker must always die.</content>
<content>But the mother of the king
hid away the feebler child, which was born the last, for her heart
yearned over it, and that child is Twala the king.</content>
<content>I am his younger
brother, born of another wife."</content>
<content>"Well?"</content>
<content>"My lord, Kafa, our father, died when we came to manhood, and my
brother Imotu was made king in his place, and for a space reigned and
had a son by his favourite wife.</content>
<content>When the babe was three years old,
just after the great war, during which no man could sow or reap, a
famine came upon the land, and the people murmured because of the
famine, and looked round like a starved lion for something to rend.</content>
<content>Then it was that Gagool, the wise and terrible woman, who does not die,
made a proclamation to the people, saying, 'The king Imotu is no king.'</content>
<content>And at the time Imotu was sick with a wound, and lay in his kraal not
able to move.</content>
<content>"Then Gagool went into a hut and led out Twala, my half-brother, and
twin brother to the king, whom she had hidden among the caves and rocks
since he was born, and stripping the ' moocha '  waist-cloth  off his
loins, showed the people of the Kukuanas the mark of the sacred snake
coiled round his middle, wherewith the eldest son of the king is marked
at birth, and cried out loud, 'Behold your king whom I have saved for
you even to this day!'</content>
<content>"Now the people being mad with hunger, and altogether bereft of reason
and the knowledge of truth, cried out - ' The king!</content>
<content>The king! '</content>
<content>but I
knew that it was not so, for Imotu my brother was the elder of the
twins, and our lawful king.</content>
<content>Then just as the tumult was at its height
Imotu the king, though he was very sick, crawled from his hut holding
his wife by the hand, and followed by his little son Ignosi - that is,
by interpretation, the Lightning.</content>
<content>"'What is this noise?'</content>
<content>he asked.</content>
<content>'Why cry ye The king!</content>
<content>The king! '</content>
<content>"Then Twala, his twin brother, born of the same woman, and in the same
hour, ran to him, and taking him by the hair, stabbed him through the
heart with his knife.</content>
<content>And the people being fickle, and ever ready to
worship the rising sun, clapped their hands and cried, ' Twala is
king!</content>
<content>Now we know that Twala is king!'"</content>
<content>"And what became of Imotu's wife and her son Ignosi?</content>
<content>Did Twala kill
them too?"</content>
<content>"Nay, my lord.</content>
<content>When she saw that her lord was dead the queen seized the
child with a cry and ran away.</content>
<content>Two days afterward she came to a kraal
very hungry, and none would give her milk or food, now that her lord
the king was dead, for all men hate the unfortunate.</content>
<content>But at nightfall a
little child, a girl, crept out and brought her corn to eat, and she
blessed the child, and went on towards the mountains with her boy
before the sun rose again, and there she must have perished, for none
have seen her since, nor the child Ignosi."</content>
<content>"Then if this child Ignosi had lived he would be the true king of the
Kukuana people?"</content>
<content>"That is so, my lord; the sacred snake is round his middle.</content>
<content>If he lives
he is king; but, alas!</content>
<content>he is long dead."</content>
<content>"See, my lord," and Infadoos pointed to a vast collection of huts
surrounded by a fence, which was in its turn encircled by a great
ditch, that lay on the plain beneath us.</content>
<content>"That is the kraal where the
wife of Imotu was last seen with the child Ignosi.</content>
<content>It is there that we
shall sleep to-night, if, indeed," he added doubtfully, "my lords sleep
at all upon this earth."</content>
<content>"When we are among the Kukuanas, my good friend Infadoos, we do as the
Kukuanas do," I said majestically, and turned round quickly to address
Good, who was tramping along sullenly behind, his mind fully occupied
with unsatisfactory attempts to prevent his flannel shirt from flapping
in the evening breeze.</content>
<content>To my astonishment I butted into Umbopa, who was
walking along immediately behind me, and very evidently had been
listening with the greatest interest to my conversation with Infadoos.</content>
<content>The expression on his face was most curious, and gave me the idea of a
man who was struggling with partial success to bring something long ago
forgotten back into his mind.</content>
<content>All this while we had been pressing on at a good rate towards the
undulating plain beneath us.</content>
<content>The mountains we had crossed now loomed
high above our heads, and Sheba's Breasts were veiled modestly in
diaphanous wreaths of mist.</content>
<content>As we went the country grew more and more
lovely.</content>
<content>The vegetation was luxuriant, without being tropical; the sun
was bright and warm, but not burning; and a gracious breeze blew softly
along the odorous slopes of the mountains.</content>
<content>Indeed, this new land was
little less than an earthly paradise; in beauty, in natural wealth, and
in climate I have never seen its like.</content>
<content>The Transvaal is a fine country,
but it is nothing to Kukuanaland.</content>
<content>So soon as we started Infadoos had despatched a runner to warn the
people of the kraal, which, by the way, was in his military command, of
our arrival.</content>
<content>This man had departed at an extraordinary speed, which
Infadoos informed me he would keep up all the way, as running was an
exercise much practised among his people.</content>
<content>The result of this message now became apparent.</content>
<content>When we arrived within
two miles of the kraal we could see that company after company of men
were issuing from its gates and marching towards us.</content>
<content>Sir Henry laid his hand upon my arm, and remarked that it looked as
though we were going to meet with a warm reception.</content>
<content>Something in his
tone attracted Infadoos' attention.</content>
<content>"Let not my lords be afraid," he said hastily, "for in my breast there
dwells no guile.</content>
<content>This regiment is one under my command, and comes out
by my orders to greet you."</content>
<content>I nodded easily, though I was not quite easy in my mind.</content>
<content>About half a mile from the gates of this kraal is a long stretch of
rising ground sloping gently upwards from the road, and here the
companies formed.</content>
<content>It was a splendid sight to see them, each company
about three hundred strong, charging swiftly up the rise, with flashing
spears and waving plumes, to take their appointed place.</content>
<content>By the time we
reached the slope twelve such companies, or in all three thousand six
hundred men, had passed out and taken up their positions along the road.</content>
<content>Presently we came to the first company, and were able to gaze in
astonishment on the most magnificent set of warriors that I have ever
seen.</content>
<content>They were all men of mature age, mostly veterans of about forty,
and not one of them was under six feet in height, whilst many stood six
feet three or four.</content>
<content>They wore upon their heads heavy black plumes of
Sakaboola feathers, like those which adorned our guides.</content>
<content>About their
waists and beneath the right knees were bound circlets of white ox
tails, while in their left hands they carried round shields measuring
about twenty inches across.</content>
<content>These shields are very curious.</content>
<content>The
framework is made of an iron plate beaten out thin, over which is
stretched milk-white ox-hide.</content>
<content>The weapons that each man bore were simple, but most effective,
consisting of a short and very heavy two-edged spear with a wooden
shaft, the blade being about six inches across at the widest part.</content>
<content>These spears are not used for throwing but like the Zulu " bangwan ,"
or stabbing assegai, are for close quarters only, when the wound
inflicted by them is terrible.</content>
<content>In addition to his bangwan every man
carried three large and heavy knives, each knife weighing about two
pounds.</content>
<content>One knife was fixed in the ox-tail girdle, and the other two at
the back of the round shield.</content>
<content>These knives, which are called " tollas "
by the Kukuanas, take the place of the throwing assegai of the Zulus.</content>
<content>The Kukuana warriors can cast them with great accuracy to a distance of
fifty yards, and it is their custom on charging to hurl a volley of
them at the enemy as they come to close quarters.</content>
<content>Each company remained still as a collection of bronze statues till we
were opposite to it, when at a signal given by its commanding officer,
who, distinguished by a leopard skin cloak, stood some paces in front,
every spear was raised into the air, and from three hundred throats
sprang forth with a sudden roar the royal salute of " Koom ."</content>
<content>Then, so
soon as we had passed, the company formed up behind us and followed us
towards the kraal, till at last the whole regiment of the "Greys" - so
called from their white shields - the crack corps of the Kukuana people,
was marching in our rear with a tread that shook the ground.</content>
<content>At length, branching off from Solomon's Great Road, we came to the wide
fosse surrounding the kraal, which is at least a mile round, and fenced
with a strong palisade of piles formed of the trunks of trees.</content>
<content>At the
gateway this fosse is spanned by a primitive drawbridge, which was let
down by the guard to allow us to pass in.</content>
<content>The kraal is exceedingly well
laid out.</content>
<content>Through the centre runs a wide pathway intersected at right
angles by other pathways so arranged as to cut the huts into square
blocks, each block being the quarters of a company.</content>
<content>The huts are
dome-shaped, and built, like those of the Zulus, of a framework of
wattle, beautifully thatched with grass; but, unlike the Zulu huts,
they have doorways through which men could walk.</content>
<content>Also they are much
larger, and surrounded by a verandah about six feet wide, beautifully
paved with powdered lime trodden hard.</content>
<content>All along each side of this wide pathway that pierces the kraal were
ranged hundreds of women, brought out by curiosity to look at us.</content>
<content>These
women, for a native race, are exceedingly handsome.</content>
<content>They are tall and
graceful, and their figures are wonderfully fine.</content>
<content>The hair, though
short, is rather curly than woolly, the features are frequently
aquiline, and the lips are not unpleasantly thick, as is the case among
most African races.</content>
<content>But what struck us most was their exceedingly quiet
and dignified air.</content>
<content>They were as well-bred in their way as the
 habituÃ©es of a fashionable drawing-room, and in this respect they
differ from Zulu women and their cousins the Masai who inhabit the
district beyond Zanzibar.</content>
<content>Their curiosity had brought them out to see
us, but they allowed no rude expressions of astonishment or savage
criticism to pass their lips as we trudged wearily in front of them.</content>
<content>Not even when old Infadoos with a surreptitious motion of the hand
pointed out the crowning wonder of poor Good's "beautiful white legs,"
did they suffer the feeling of intense admiration which evidently
mastered their minds to find expression.</content>
<content>They fixed their dark eyes
upon this new and snowy loveliness, for, as I think I have said, Good's
skin is exceedingly white, and that was all.</content>
<content>But it was quite enough
for Good, who is modest by nature.</content>
<content>When we reached the centre of the kraal, Infadoos halted at the door of
a large hut, which was surrounded at a distance by a circle of smaller
ones.</content>
<content>"Enter, Sons of the Stars," he said, in a magniloquent voice, "and
deign to rest awhile in our humble habitations.</content>
<content>A little food shall be
brought to you, so that ye may have no need to draw your belts tight
from hunger; some honey and some milk, and an ox or two, and a few
sheep; not much, my lords, but still a little food."</content>
<content>"It is good," said I.</content>
<content>"Infadoos; we are weary with travelling through
realms of air; now let us rest."</content>
<content>Accordingly we entered the hut, which we found amply prepared for our
comfort.</content>
<content>Couches of tanned skins were spread for us to lie on, and
water was placed for us to wash in.</content>
<content>Presently we heard a shouting outside, and stepping to the door, saw a
line of damsels bearing milk and roasted mealies, and honey in a pot.</content>
<content>Behind these were some youths driving a fat young ox.</content>
<content>We received the
gifts, and then one of the young men drew the knife from his girdle and
dexterously cut the ox's throat.</content>
<content>In ten minutes it was dead, skinned,
and jointed.</content>
<content>The best of the meat was then cut off for us, and the
rest, in the name of our party, I presented to the warriors round us,
who took it and distributed the "white lords' gift."</content>
<content>Umbopa set to work, with the assistance of an extremely prepossessing
young woman, to boil our portion in a large earthenware pot over a fire
which was built outside the hut, and when it was nearly ready we sent a
message to Infadoos, and asked him and Scragga, the king's son, to join
us.</content>
<content>Presently they came, and sitting down upon little stools, of which
there were several about the hut, for the Kukuanas do not in general
squat upon their haunches like the Zulus, they helped us to get through
our dinner.</content>
<content>The old gentleman was most affable and polite, but it
struck me that the young one regarded us with doubt.</content>
<content>Together with the
rest of the party, he had been overawed by our white appearance and by
our magic properties; but it seemed to me that, on discovering that we
ate, drank, and slept like other mortals, his awe was beginning to wear
off, and to be replaced by a sullen suspicion - which made me feel
rather uncomfortable.</content>
<content>In the course of our meal Sir Henry suggested to me that it might be
well to try to discover if our hosts knew anything of his brother's
fate, or if they had ever seen or heard of him; but, on the whole, I
thought that it would be wiser to say nothing of the matter at this
time.</content>
<content>It was difficult to explain a relative lost from "the Stars."</content>
<content>After supper we produced our pipes and lit them; a proceeding which
filled Infadoos and Scragga with astonishment.</content>
<content>The Kukuanas were
evidently unacquainted with the divine delights of tobacco-smoke.</content>
<content>The
herb is grown among them extensively; but, like the Zulus, they use it
for snuff only, and quite failed to identify it in its new form.</content>
<content>Presently I asked Infadoos when we were to proceed on our journey, and
was delighted to learn that preparations had been made for us to leave
on the following morning, messengers having already departed to inform
Twala the king of our coming.</content>
<content>It appeared that Twala was at his principal place, known as Loo, making
ready for the great annual feast which was to be held in the first week
of June.</content>
<content>At this gathering all the regiments, with the exception of
certain detachments left behind for garrison purposes, are brought up
and paraded before the king; and the great annual witch-hunt, of which
more by-and-by, is held.</content>
<content>We were to start at dawn; and Infadoos, who was to accompany us,
expected that we should reach Loo on the night of the second day,
unless we were detained by accident or by swollen rivers.</content>
<content>When they had given us this information our visitors bade us
good-night; and, having arranged to watch turn and turn about, three of
us flung ourselves down and slept the sweet sleep of the weary, whilst
the fourth sat up on the look-out for possible treachery.</content>
<content>CHAPTER IX

TWALA THE KING

It will not be necessary for me to detail at length the incidents of
our journey to Loo.</content>
<content>It took two full days' travelling along Solomon's
Great Road, which pursued its even course right into the heart of
Kukuanaland.</content>
<content>Suffice it to say that as we went the country seemed to
grow richer and richer, and the kraals, with their wide surrounding
belts of cultivation, more and more numerous.</content>
<content>They were all built upon
the same principles as the first camp which we had reached, and were
guarded by ample garrisons of troops.</content>
<content>Indeed, in Kukuanaland, as among
the Germans, the Zulus, and the Masai, every able-bodied man is a
soldier, so that the whole force of the nation is available for its
wars, offensive or defensive.</content>
<content>As we travelled we were overtaken by
thousands of warriors hurrying up to Loo to be present at the great
annual review and festival, and more splendid troops I never saw.</content>
<content>At sunset on the second day, we stopped to rest awhile upon the summit
of some heights over which the road ran, and there on a beautiful and
fertile plain before us lay Loo itself.</content>
<content>For a native town it is an
enormous place, quite five miles round, I should say, with outlying
kraals projecting from it, that serve on grand occasions as cantonments
for the regiments, and a curious horseshoe-shaped hill, with which we
were destined to become better acquainted, about two miles to the
north.</content>
<content>It is beautifully situated, and through the centre of the kraal,
dividing it into two portions, runs a river, which appeared to be
bridged in several places, the same indeed that we had seen from the
slopes of Sheba's Breasts.</content>
<content>Sixty or seventy miles away three great
snow-capped mountains, placed at the points of a triangle, started out
of the level plain.</content>
<content>The conformation of these mountains is unlike that
of Sheba's Breasts, being sheer and precipitous, instead of smooth and
rounded.</content>
<content>Infadoos saw us looking at them, and volunteered a remark.</content>
<content>"The road ends there," he said, pointing to the mountains known among
the Kukuanas as the "Three Witches."</content>
<content>"Why does it end?"</content>
<content>I asked.</content>
<content>"Who knows?"</content>
<content>he answered with a shrug; "the mountains are full of
caves, and there is a great pit between them.</content>
<content>It is there that the wise
men of old time used to go to get whatever it was they came for to this
country, and it is there now that our kings are buried in the Place of
Death."</content>
<content>"What was it they came for?"</content>
<content>I asked eagerly.</content>
<content>"Nay, I know not.</content>
<content>My lords who have dropped from the Stars should
know," he answered with a quick look.</content>
<content>Evidently he knew more than he
chose to say.</content>
<content>"Yes," I went on, "you are right, in the Stars we learn many things.</content>
<content>I
have heard, for instance, that the wise men of old came to these
mountains to find bright stones, pretty playthings, and yellow iron."</content>
<content>"My lord is wise," he answered coldly; "I am but a child and cannot
talk with my lord on such matters.</content>
<content>My lord must speak with Gagool the
old, at the king's place, who is wise even as my lord," and he went
away.</content>
<content>So soon as he was gone I turned to the others, and pointed out the
mountains.</content>
<content>"There are Solomon's diamond mines," I said.</content>
<content>Umbopa was standing with them, apparently plunged in one of the fits of
abstraction which were common to him, and caught my words.</content>
<content>"Yes, Macumazahn," he put in, in Zulu, "the diamonds are surely there,
and you shall have them, since you white men are so fond of toys and
money."</content>
<content>"How dost thou know that, Umbopa?"</content>
<content>I asked sharply, for I did not like
his mysterious ways.</content>
<content>He laughed.</content>
<content>"I dreamed it in the night, white men;" then he too turned
on his heel and went.</content>
<content>"Now what," said Sir Henry, "is our black friend driving at?</content>
<content>He knows
more than he chooses to say, that is clear.</content>
<content>By the way, Quatermain, has
he heard anything of - of my brother?"</content>
<content>"Nothing; he has asked everyone he has become friendly with, but they
all declare that no white man has ever been seen in the country before."</content>
<content>"Do you suppose that he got here at all?"</content>
<content>suggested Good; "we have only
reached the place by a miracle; is it likely he could have reached it
without the map?"</content>
<content>"I don't  know," said Sir Henry gloomily, "but somehow I think that I
shall find him."</content>
<content>Slowly the sun sank, then suddenly darkness rushed down on the land
like a tangible thing.</content>
<content>There was no breathing-space between the day and
night, no soft transformation scene, for in these latitudes twilight
does not exist.</content>
<content>The change from day to night is as quick and as
absolute as the change from life to death.</content>
<content>The sun sank and the world
was wreathed in shadows.</content>
<content>But not for long, for see in the west there is
a glow, then come rays of silver light, and at last the full and
glorious moon lights up the plain and shoots its gleaming arrows far
and wide, filling the earth with a faint refulgence.</content>
<content>We stood and watched the lovely sight, whilst the stars grew pale
before this chastened majesty, and felt our hearts lifted up in the
presence of a beauty that I cannot describe.</content>
<content>Mine has been a rough
life, but there are a few things I am thankful to have lived for, and
one of them is to have seen that moon shine over Kukuanaland.</content>
<content>Presently our meditations were broken in upon by our polite friend
Infadoos.</content>
<content>"If my lords are rested we will journey on to Loo, where a hut is made
ready for my lords to-night.</content>
<content>The moon is now bright, so that we shall
not fall by the way."</content>
<content>We assented, and in an hour's time were at the outskirts of the town,
of which the extent, mapped out as it was by thousands of camp fires,
appeared absolutely endless.</content>
<content>Indeed, Good, who is always fond of a bad
joke, christened it "Unlimited Loo."</content>
<content>Soon we came to a moat with a
drawbridge, where we were met by the rattling of arms and the hoarse
challenge of a sentry.</content>
<content>Infadoos gave some password that I could not
catch, which was met with a salute, and we passed on through the
central street of the great grass city.</content>
<content>After nearly half an hour's
tramp, past endless lines of huts, Infadoos halted at last by the gate
of a little group of huts which surrounded a small courtyard of
powdered limestone, and informed us that these were to be our "poor"
quarters.</content>
<content>We entered, and found that a hut had been assigned to each of us.</content>
<content>These
huts were superior to any that we had yet seen, and in each was a most
comfortable bed made of tanned skins, spread upon mattresses of
aromatic grass.</content>
<content>Food too was ready for us, and so soon as we had washed
ourselves with water, which stood ready in earthenware jars, some young
women of handsome appearance brought us roasted meats, and mealie cobs
daintily served on wooden platters, and presented them to us with deep
obeisances.</content>
<content>We ate and drank, and then, the beds having been all moved into one hut
by our request, a precaution at which the amiable young ladies smiled,
we flung ourselves down to sleep, thoroughly wearied with our long
journey.</content>
<content>When we woke it was to find the sun high in the heavens, and the female
attendants, who did not seem to be troubled by any false shame, already
standing inside the hut, having been ordered to attend and help us to
"make ready."</content>
<content>"Make ready, indeed," growled Good; "when one has only a flannel shirt
and a pair of boots, that does not take long.</content>
<content>I wish you would ask them
for my trousers, Quatermain."</content>
<content>I asked accordingly, but was informed that these sacred relics had
already been taken to the king, who would see us in the forenoon.</content>
<content>Somewhat to their astonishment and disappointment, having requested the
young ladies to step outside, we proceeded to make the best toilet of
which the circumstances admitted.</content>
<content>Good even went the length of again
shaving the right side of his face; the left, on which now appeared a
very fair crop of whiskers, we impressed upon him he must on no account
touch.</content>
<content>As for ourselves, we were contented with a good wash and combing
our hair.</content>
<content>Sir Henry's yellow locks were now almost upon his shoulders,
and he looked more like an ancient Dane than ever, while my grizzled
scrub was fully an inch long, instead of half an inch, which in a
general way I considered my maximum length.</content>
<content>By the time that we had eaten our breakfast, and smoked a pipe, a
message was brought to us by no less a personage than Infadoos himself
that Twala the king was ready to see us, if we would be pleased to come.</content>
<content>We remarked in reply that we should prefer to wait till the sun was a
little higher, we were yet weary with our journey,  c.,  c. It is
always well, when dealing with uncivilised people, not to be in too
great a hurry.</content>
<content>They are apt to mistake politeness for awe or servility.</content>
<content>So, although we were quite as anxious to see Twala as Twala could be to
see us, we sat down and waited for an hour, employing the interval in
preparing such presents as our slender stock of goods
permitted - namely, the Winchester rifle which had been used by poor
Ventv"gel, and some beads.</content>
<content>The rifle and ammunition we determined to
present to his royal highness, and the beads were for his wives and
courtiers.</content>
<content>We had already given a few to Infadoos and Scragga, and
found that they were delighted with them, never having seen such things
before.</content>
<content>At length we declared that we were ready, and guided by
Infadoos, started off to the audience, Umbopa carrying the rifle and
beads.</content>
<content>After walking a few hundred yards we came to an enclosure, something
like that surrounding the huts which had been allotted to us, only
fifty times as big, for it could not have covered less than six or
seven acres of ground.</content>
<content>All round the outside fence stood a row of huts,
which were the habitations of the king's wives.</content>
<content>Exactly opposite the
gateway, on the further side of the open space, was a very large hut,
built by itself, in which his majesty resided.</content>
<content>All the rest was open
ground; that is to say, it would have been open had it not been filled
by company after company of warriors, who were mustered there to the
number of seven or eight thousand.</content>
<content>These men stood still as statues as
we advanced through them, and it would be impossible to give an
adequate idea of the grandeur of the spectacle which they presented,
with their waving plumes, their glancing spears, and iron-backed
ox-hide shields.</content>
<content>The space in front of the large hut was empty, but before it were
placed several stools.</content>
<content>On three of these, at a sign from Infadoos, we
seated ourselves, Umbopa standing behind us.</content>
<content>As for Infadoos, he took
up a position by the door of the hut.</content>
<content>So we waited for ten minutes or
more in the midst of a dead silence, but conscious that we were the
object of the concentrated gaze of some eight thousand pairs of eyes.</content>
<content>It was a somewhat trying ordeal, but we carried it off as best we
could.</content>
<content>At length the door of the hut opened, and a gigantic figure,
with a splendid tiger-skin karross flung over its shoulders, stepped
out, followed by the boy Scragga, and what appeared to us to be a
withered-up monkey, wrapped in a fur cloak.</content>
<content>The figure seated itself
upon a stool, Scragga took his stand behind it, and the withered-up
monkey crept on all fours into the shade of the hut and squatted down.</content>
<content>Still there was silence.</content>
<content>Then the gigantic figure slipped off the karross and stood up before
us, a truly alarming spectacle.</content>
<content>It was that of an enormous man with the
most entirely repulsive countenance we had ever beheld.</content>
<content>This man's lips
were as thick as a Negro's, the nose was flat, he had but one gleaming
black eye, for the other was represented by a hollow in the face, and
his whole expression was cruel and sensual to a degree.</content>
<content>From the large
head rose a magnificent plume of white ostrich feathers, his body was
clad in a shirt of shining chain armour, whilst round the waist and
right knee were the usual garnishes of white ox-tail.</content>
<content>In his right hand
was a huge spear, about the neck a thick torque of gold, and bound on
the forehead shone dully a single and enormous uncut diamond.</content>
<content>Still there was silence; but not for long.</content>
<content>Presently the man, whom we
rightly guessed to be the king, raised the great javelin in his hand.</content>
<content>Instantly eight thousand spears were lifted in answer, and from eight
thousand throats rang out the royal salute of " Koom ."</content>
<content>Three times
this was repeated, and each time the earth shook with the noise, that
can only be compared to the deepest notes of thunder.</content>
<content>"Be humble, O people," piped out a thin voice which seemed to come from
the monkey in the shade, "it is the king."</content>
<content>" It is the king ," boomed out the eight thousand throats in answer.
"</content>
<content>Be humble, O people, it is the king. "</content>
<content>Then there was silence again - dead silence.</content>
<content>Presently, however, it was
broken.</content>
<content>A soldier on our left dropped his shield, which fell with a
clatter on to the limestone flooring.</content>
<content>Twala turned his one cold eye in the direction of the noise.</content>
<content>"Come hither, thou," he said, in a cold voice.</content>
<content>A fine young man stepped out of the ranks, and stood before him.</content>
<content>"It was thy shield that fell, thou awkward dog.</content>
<content>Wilt thou make me a
reproach in the eyes of these strangers from the Stars?</content>
<content>What hast thou
to say for thyself?"</content>
<content>We saw the poor fellow turn pale under his dusky skin.</content>
<content>"It was by chance, O Calf of the Black Cow," he murmured.</content>
<content>"Then it is a chance for which thou must pay.</content>
<content>Thou hast made me
foolish; prepare for death."</content>
<content>"I am the king's ox," was the low answer.</content>
<content>"Scragga," roared the king, "let me see how thou canst use thy spear.</content>
<content>Kill me this blundering fool."</content>
<content>Scragga stepped forward with an ill-favoured grin, and lifted his
spear.</content>
<content>The poor victim covered his eyes with his hand and stood still.</content>
<content>As for us, we were petrified with horror.</content>
<content>"Once, twice," he waved the spear, and then struck, ah!</content>
<content>right home - the
spear stood out a foot behind the soldier's back.</content>
<content>He flung up his hands
and dropped dead.</content>
<content>From the multitude about us rose something like a
murmur, it rolled round and round, and died away.</content>
<content>The tragedy was
finished; there lay the corpse, and we had not yet realised that it had
been enacted.</content>
<content>Sir Henry sprang up and swore a great oath, then,
overpowered by the sense of silence, sat down again.</content>
<content>"The thrust was a good one," said the king; "take him away."</content>
<content>Four men stepped out of the ranks, and lifting the body of the murdered
man, carried it thence.</content>
<content>"Cover up the blood-stains, cover them up," piped out the thin voice
that proceeded from the monkey-like figure; "the king's word is spoken,
the king's doom is done!"</content>
<content>Thereupon a girl came forward from behind the hut, bearing a jar filled
with powdered lime, which she scattered over the red mark, blotting it
from sight.</content>
<content>Sir Henry meanwhile was boiling with rage at what had happened; indeed,
it was with difficulty that we could keep him still.</content>
<content>"Sit down, for heaven's sake," I whispered; "our lives depend on it."</content>
<content>He yielded and remained quiet.</content>
<content>Twala sat silent until the traces of the tragedy had been removed, then
he addressed us.</content>
<content>"White people," he said, "who come hither, whence I know not, and why I
know not, greeting."</content>
<content>"Greeting, Twala, King of the Kukuanas," I answered.</content>
<content>"White people, whence come ye, and what seek ye?"</content>
<content>"We come from the Stars, ask us not how.</content>
<content>We come to see this land."</content>
<content>"Ye journey from far to see a little thing.</content>
<content>And that man with you,"
pointing to Umbopa, "does he also come from the Stars?"</content>
<content>"Even so; there are people of thy colour in the heavens above; but ask
not of matters too high for thee, Twala the king."</content>
<content>"Ye speak with a loud voice, people of the Stars," Twala answered in a
tone which I scarcely liked.</content>
<content>"Remember that the Stars are far off, and
ye are here.</content>
<content>How if I make you as him whom they bore away?"</content>
<content>I laughed out loud, though there was little laughter in my heart.</content>
<content>"O king," I said, "be careful, walk warily over hot stones, lest thou
shouldst burn thy feet; hold the spear by the handle, lest thou should
cut thy hands.</content>
<content>Touch but one hair of our heads, and destruction shall
come upon thee.</content>
<content>What, have not these" - pointing to Infadoos and
Scragga, who, young villain that he was, was employed in cleaning the
blood of the soldier off his spear - "told thee what manner of men we
are?</content>
<content>Hast thou seen the like of us?"</content>
<content>and I pointed to Good, feeling
quite sure that he had never seen anybody before who looked in the
least like him as he then appeared.</content>
<content>"It is true, I have not," said the king, surveying Good with interest.</content>
<content>"Have they not told thee how we strike with death from afar?"</content>
<content>I went on.</content>
<content>"They have told me, but I believe them not.</content>
<content>Let me see you kill.</content>
<content>Kill
me a man among those who stand yonder" - and he pointed to the opposite
side of the kraal - "and I will believe."</content>
<content>"Nay," I answered; "we shed no blood of men except in just punishment;
but if thou wilt see, bid thy servants drive in an ox through the kraal
gates, and before he has run twenty paces I will strike him dead."</content>
<content>"Nay," laughed the king, "kill me a man and I will believe."</content>
<content>"Good, O king, so be it," I answered coolly; "do thou walk across the
open space, and before thy feet reach the gate thou shalt be dead; or
if thou wilt not, send thy son Scragga"  whom at that moment it would
have given me much pleasure to shoot .</content>
<content>On hearing this suggestion Scragga uttered a sort of howl, and bolted
into the hut.</content>
<content>Twala frowned majestically; the suggestion did not please him.</content>
<content>"Let a young ox be driven in," he said.</content>
<content>Two men at once departed, running swiftly.</content>
<content>"Now, Sir Henry," said I, "do you shoot.</content>
<content>I want to show this ruffian
that I am not the only magician of the party."</content>
<content>Sir Henry accordingly took his "express," and made ready.</content>
<content>"I hope I shall make a good shot," he groaned.</content>
<content>"You must," I answered.</content>
<content>"If you miss with the first barrel, let him
have the second.</content>
<content>Sight for 150 yards, and wait till the beast turns
broadside on."</content>
<content>Then came a pause, until presently we caught sight of an ox running
straight for the kraal gate.</content>
<content>It came on through the gate, then,

<content>Up went the rifle.</content>
<content>Bang!</content>

<content>The

<content>I turned round coolly - 

"Have I lied, O king?"</content>
<content>"Nay, white man, it is the truth," was the somewhat awed answer.</content>
<content>"Listen, Twala," I went on.</content>
<content>"Thou hast seen.</content>
<content>Now know we come in peace,

<content>In a few seconds it was done.</content>
<content>"Now, see, I will break yonder spear."</content>

<content>The bullet struck the flat of the

<content>Again the sigh of astonishment went up.</content>
<content>"Now, Twala, we give this magic tube to thee, and by-and-by I will show

<content>The king took it very gingerly, and laid it down at his feet.</content>
<content>As he did

<content>It crept on all fours, but when it reached the place where

<content>Apparently it was that of a woman of great age so shrunken that in size

<content>Set in these wrinkles was a

<content>There was no nose to speak of; indeed, the visage

<content>As

<content>Then suddenly it projected a

<content>CHAPTER X

THE WITCH-HUNT

On reaching our hut I motioned to Infadoos to enter with us.</content>
<content>"Now, Infadoos," I said, "we would speak with thee."</content>
<content>"Let my lords say on."</content>
<content>"It seems to us, Infadoos, that Twala the king is a cruel man."</content>
<content>"It is so, my lords.</content>
<content>Alas!</content>
<content>the land cries out because of his cruelties.</content>
<content>To-night ye shall see.</content>
<content>It is the great witch-hunt, and many will be
smelt out as wizards and slain.</content>
<content>No man's life is safe.</content>
<content>If the king
covets a man's cattle, or a man's wife, or if he fears a man that he

some of the witch-finding women whom she has taught, will smell that
man out as a wizard, and he will be killed.</content>
<content>Many must die before the
moon grows pale to-night.</content>
<content>It is ever so.</content>
<content>Perhaps I too shall be killed.</content>
<content>As yet I have been spared because I am skilled in war, and am beloved
by the soldiers; but I know not how long I have to live.</content>
<content>The land
groans at the cruelties of Twala the king; it is wearied of him and his
red ways."</content>
<content>"Then why is it, Infadoos, that the people do not cast him down?"</content>
<content>"Nay, my lords, he is the king, and if he were killed Scragga would

<content>If Scragga were king his yoke upon our neck would
be heavier than the yoke of Twala.</content>
<content>If Imotu had never been slain, or if
Ignosi his son had lived, it might have been otherwise; but they are

<content>said a voice behind us.</content>

<content>asked Infadoos; "who told thee to speak?"</content>
<content>"Listen, Infadoos," was the answer, "and I will tell thee a story.</content>
<content>Years ago the king Imotu was killed in this country and his wife fled

<content>Is it not so?"</content>
<content>"It is so."</content>
<content>"It was said that the woman and her son died upon the mountains.</content>
<content>Is it

<content>They crossed the mountains and were led by a tribe of wandering desert

<content>"How knowest thou this?"</content>
<content>"Listen.</content>
<content>They travelled on and on, many months' journey, till they

<content>Then the son Ignosi became a wanderer again,

<content>"For years he lived there working as a servant and a soldier, but

<content>For long years he

<content>The remainder of that day we spent quietly, resting and talking over

<content>At last the sun went

<content>Then the full moon shone out in splendour, and as we stood

<content>As he

<content>These steel shirts, which evidently had been made for

<content>These regiments were in

<content>Wherever we

<content>One third is

<content>Thou seest it is a great people."</content>

follow the words, except in so far as they appeared to represent

<content>Now it seemed to be
a love song, now a majestic swelling war chant, and last of all a death
dirge ending suddenly in one heart-breaking wail that went echoing and
rolling away in a volume of blood-curdling sound.</content>
<content>Again silence fell upon the place, and again it was broken by the king
lifting his hand.</content>
<content>Instantly we heard a pattering of feet, and from out
of the masses of warriors strange and awful figures appeared running
towards us.</content>
<content>As they drew near we saw that these were women, most of
them aged, for their white hair, ornamented with small bladders taken
from fish, streamed out behind them.</content>
<content>Their faces were painted in
stripes of white and yellow; down their backs hung snake-skins, and
round their waists rattled circlets of human bones, while each held a
small forked wand in her shrivelled hand.</content>
<content>In all there were ten of
them.</content>
<content>When they arrived in front of us they halted, and one of them,
pointing with her wand towards the crouching figure of Gagool, cried
out - 

"Mother, old mother, we are here."</content>
<content>" Good!</content>
<content>good!</content>
<content>good! "</content>
<content>answered that aged Iniquity.</content>
<content>"Are your eyes keen,
 Isanusis  witch doctresses , ye seers in dark places?"</content>
<content>"Mother, they are keen."</content>
<content>" Good!</content>
<content>good!</content>
<content>good!</content>
<content>Are your ears open, Isanusis , ye who hear words
that come not from the tongue?"</content>
<content>"Mother, they are open."</content>
<content>" Good!</content>
<content>good!</content>
<content>good!</content>
<content>Are your senses awake, Isanusis - can ye smell
blood, can ye purge the land of the wicked ones who compass evil
against the king and against their neighbours?</content>
<content>Are ye ready to do the
justice of 'Heaven above,' ye whom I have taught, who have eaten of the
bread of my wisdom, and drunk of the water of my magic?"</content>
<content>"Mother, we can."</content>
<content>"Then go!</content>
<content>Tarry not, ye vultures; see, the slayers" - pointing to the
ominous group of executioners behind - "make sharp their spears; the
white men from afar are hungry to see.</content>
<content>Go! "</content>
<content>With a wild yell Gagool's horrid ministers broke away in every
direction, like fragments from a shell, the dry bones round their
waists rattling as they ran, and headed for various points of the dense
human circle.</content>
<content>We could not watch them all, so we fixed our eyes upon
the Isanusi nearest to us.</content>
<content>When she came to within a few paces of the
warriors she halted and began to dance wildly, turning round and round
with an almost incredible rapidity, and shrieking out sentences such as
"I smell him, the evil-doer!"</content>
<content>"He is near, he who poisoned his mother!"</content>
<content>"I hear the thoughts of him who thought evil of the king!"</content>
<content>Quicker and quicker she danced, till she lashed herself into such a
frenzy of excitement that the foam flew in specks from her gnashing
jaws, till her eyes seemed to start from her head, and her flesh to
quiver visibly.</content>
<content>Suddenly she stopped dead and stiffened all over, like
a pointer dog when he scents game, and then with outstretched wand she
began to creep stealthily towards the soldiers before her.</content>
<content>It seemed to
us that as she came their stoicism gave way, and that they shrank from
her.</content>
<content>As for ourselves, we followed her movements with a horrible
fascination.</content>
<content>Presently, still creeping and crouching like a dog, the
 Isanusi was before them.</content>
<content>Then she halted and pointed, and again crept
on a pace or two.</content>
<content>Suddenly the end came.</content>
<content>With a shriek she sprang in and touched a tall
warrior with her forked wand.</content>
<content>Instantly two of his comrades, those
standing immediately next to him, seized the doomed man, each by one
arm, and advanced with him towards the king.</content>
<content>He did not resist, but we saw that he dragged his limbs as though they
were paralysed, and that his fingers, from which the spear had fallen,
were limp like those of a man newly dead.</content>
<content>As he came, two of the villainous executioners stepped forward to meet
him.</content>
<content>Presently they met, and the executioners turned round, looking
towards the king as though for orders.

"</content>
<content>Kill! "</content>
<content>said the king.

"</content>
<content>Kill! "</content>
<content>squeaked Gagool.

"</content>
<content>Kill! "</content>
<content>re-echoed Scragga, with a hollow chuckle.</content>
<content>Almost before the words were uttered the horrible dead was done.</content>
<content>One
man had driven his spear into the victim's heart, and to make assurance
double sure, the other had dashed out his brains with a great club.

"</content>
<content>One ," counted Twala the king, just like a black Madame Defarge, as
Good said, and the body was dragged a few paces away and stretched out.</content>
<content>Hardly was the thing done before another poor wretch was brought up,
like an ox to the slaughter.</content>
<content>This time we could see, from the
leopard-skin cloak which he wore, that the man was a person of rank.</content>
<content>Again the awful syllables were spoken, and the victim fell dead.

"</content>
<content>Two ," counted the king.</content>
<content>And so the deadly game went on, till about a hundred bodies were
stretched in rows behind us.</content>
<content>I have heard of the gladiatorial shows of
the CÃ¦sars, and of the Spanish bull-fights, but I take the liberty of
doubting if either of them could be half so horrible as this Kukuana
witch-hunt.</content>
<content>Gladiatorial shows and Spanish bull-fights at any rate
contributed to the public amusement, which certainly was not the case
here.</content>
<content>The most confirmed sensation-monger would fight shy of sensation
if he knew that it was well on the cards that he would, in his own
proper person, be the subject of the next "event."</content>
<content>Once we rose and tried to remonstrate, but were sternly repressed by
Twala.</content>
<content>"Let the law take its course, white men.</content>
<content>These dogs are magicians and
evil-doers; it is well that they should die," was the only answer
vouchsafed to us.</content>
<content>About half-past ten there was a pause.</content>
<content>The witch-finders gathered
themselves together, apparently exhausted with their bloody work, and
we thought that the performance was done with.</content>
<content>But it was not so, for
presently, to our surprise, the ancient woman, Gagool, rose from her
crouching position, and supporting herself with a stick, staggered off
into the open space.</content>
<content>It was an extraordinary sight to see this
frightful vulture-headed old creature, bent nearly double with extreme
age, gather strength by degrees, until at last she rushed about almost
as actively as her ill-omened pupils.</content>
<content>To and fro she ran, chanting to
herself, till suddenly she made a dash at a tall man standing in front
of one of the regiments, and touched him.</content>
<content>As she did this a sort of
groan went up from the regiment which evidently he commanded.</content>
<content>But two
of its officers seized him all the same, and brought him up for
execution.</content>
<content>We learned afterwards that he was a man of great wealth and
importance, being indeed a cousin of the king.</content>
<content>He was slain, and Twala counted one hundred and three.</content>
<content>Then Gagool
again sprang to and fro, gradually drawing nearer and nearer to
ourselves.</content>
<content>"Hang me if I don't  believe she is going to try her games on us,"
ejaculated Good in horror.</content>
<content>"Nonsense!"</content>
<content>said Sir Henry.</content>
<content>As for myself, when I saw that old fiend dancing nearer and nearer, my
heart positively sank into my boots.</content>
<content>I glanced behind us at the long
rows of corpses, and shivered.</content>
<content>Nearer and nearer waltzed Gagool, looking for all the world like an
animated crooked stick or comma, her horrid eyes gleaming and glowing
with a most unholy lustre.</content>
<content>Nearer she came, and yet nearer, every creature in that vast assemblage
watching her movements with intense anxiety.</content>
<content>At last she stood still
and pointed.</content>
<content>"Which is it to be?"</content>
<content>asked Sir Henry to himself.</content>
<content>In a moment all doubts were at rest, for the old hag had rushed in and
touched Umbopa, alias Ignosi, on the shoulder.</content>
<content>"I smell him out," she shrieked.</content>
<content>"Kill him, kill him, he is full of
evil; kill him, the stranger, before blood flows from him.</content>
<content>Slay him, O
king."</content>
<content>There was a pause, of which I instantly took advantage.</content>
<content>"O king," I called out, rising from my seat, "this man is the servant
of thy guests, he is their dog; whosoever sheds the blood of our dog
sheds our blood.</content>
<content>By the sacred law of hospitality I claim protection
for him."</content>
<content>"Gagool, mother of the witch-finders, has smelt him out; he must die,
white men," was the sullen answer.</content>
<content>"Nay, he shall not die," I replied; "he who tries to touch him shall
die indeed."</content>
<content>"Seize him!"</content>
<content>roared Twala to the executioners; who stood round red to
the eyes with the blood of their victims.</content>
<content>They advanced towards us, and then hesitated.</content>
<content>As for Ignosi, he
clutched his spear, and raised it as though determined to sell his life
dearly.</content>
<content>"Stand back, ye dogs!"</content>
<content>I shouted, "if ye would see to-morrow's light.</content>
<content>Touch one hair of his head and your king dies," and I covered Twala
with my revolver.</content>
<content>Sir Henry and Good also drew their pistols, Sir Henry
pointing his at the leading executioner, who was advancing to carry out
the sentence, and Good taking a deliberate aim at Gagool.</content>
<content>Twala winced perceptibly as my barrel came in a line with his broad
chest.</content>
<content>"Well," I said, "what is it to be, Twala?"</content>
<content>Then he spoke.</content>
<content>"Put away your magic tubes," he said; "ye have adjured me in the name
of hospitality, and for that reason, but not from fear of what ye can
do, I spare him.</content>
<content>Go in peace."</content>
<content>"It is well," I answered unconcernedly; "we are weary of slaughter, and
would sleep.</content>
<content>Is the dance ended?"</content>
<content>"It is ended," Twala answered sulkily.</content>
<content>"Let these dead dogs," pointing
to the long rows of corpses, "be flung out to the hyÃ¦nas and the

<content>Instantly the regiments began to defile through the kraal gateway in

<content>Then we rose also, and making our salaam to his majesty, which he
hardly deigned to acknowledge, we departed to our huts.</content>
<content>"Well," said Sir Henry, as we sat down, having first lit a lamp of the
sort used by the Kukuanas, of which the wick is made from the fibre of
a species of palm leaf, and the oil from clarified hippopotamus fat,
"well, I feel uncommonly inclined to be sick."</content>
<content>"If I had any doubts about helping Umbopa to rebel against that

<content>It was as much

<content>I tried

<content>I
wonder where Infadoos is.</content>
<content>Umbopa, my friend, you ought to be grateful

<content>"I am grateful, Bougwan," was Umbopa's answer, when I had translated,
"and I shall not forget.</content>
<content>As for Infadoos, he will be here by-and-by.</content>
<content>We
must wait."</content>
<content>So we lit our pipes and waited.</content>
