<content>CHAPTER XI

WE GIVE A SIGN

For a long while - two hours, I should think - we sat there in silence,
being too much overwhelmed by the recollection of the horrors we had
seen to talk.</content>
<content>At last, just as we were thinking of turning in - for the
night drew nigh to dawn - we heard a sound of steps.</content>
<content>Then came the
challenge of a sentry posted at the kraal gate, which apparently was
answered, though not in an audible tone, for the steps still advanced;
and in another second Infadoos had entered the hut, followed by some
half-dozen stately-looking chiefs.</content>
<content>"My lords," he said, "I have come according to my word.</content>
<content>My lords and
Ignosi, rightful king of the Kukuanas, I have brought with me these
men," pointing to the row of chiefs, "who are great men among us,
having each one of them the command of three thousand soldiers, that
live but to do their bidding, under the king's.</content>
<content>I have told them of
what I have seen, and what my ears have heard.</content>
<content>Now let them also behold
the sacred snake around thee, and hear thy story, Ignosi, that they may
say whether or no they will make cause with thee against Twala the
king."</content>
<content>By way of answer Ignosi again stripped off his girdle, and exhibited
the snake tattooed about him.</content>
<content>Each chief in turn drew near and examined
the sign by the dim light of the lamp, and without saying a word passed
on to the other side.</content>
<content>Then Ignosi resumed his moocha, and addressing them, repeated the
history he had detailed in the morning.</content>
<content>"Now ye have heard, chiefs," said Infadoos, when he had done, "what say
ye: will ye stand by this man and help him to his father's throne, or
will ye not?</content>
<content>The land cries out against Twala, and the blood of the
people flows like the waters in spring.</content>
<content>Ye have seen to-night.</content>
<content>Two
other chiefs there were with whom I had it in my mind to speak, and
where are they now?</content>
<content>The hyÃ¦nas howl over their corpses.</content>
<content>Soon shall ye
be as they are if ye strike not.</content>
<content>Choose then, my brothers."</content>
<content>The eldest of the six men, a short, thick-set warrior, with white hair,
stepped forward a pace and answered - 

"Thy words are true, Infadoos; the land cries out.</content>
<content>My own brother is
among those who died to-night; but this is a great matter, and the
thing is hard to believe.</content>
<content>How know we that if we lift our spears it may
not be for a thief and a liar?</content>
<content>It is a great matter, I say, of which
none can see the end.</content>
<content>For of this be sure, blood will flow in rivers
before the deed is done; many will still cleave to the king, for men
worship the sun that still shines bright in the heavens, rather than
that which has not risen.</content>
<content>These white men from the Stars, their magic
is great, and Ignosi is under the cover of their wing.</content>
<content>If he be indeed
the rightful king, let them give us a sign, and let the people have a
sign, that all may see.</content>
<content>So shall men cleave to us, knowing of a truth
that the white man's magic is with them."</content>
<content>"Ye have the sign of the snake," I answered.</content>
<content>"My lord, it is not enough.</content>
<content>The snake may have been placed there since
the man's childhood.</content>
<content>Show us a sign, and it will suffice.</content>
<content>But we will
not move without a sign."</content>
<content>The others gave a decided assent, and I turned in perplexity to Sir
Henry and Good, and explained the situation.</content>
<content>"I think that I have it," said Good exultingly; "ask them to give us a
moment to think."</content>
<content>I did so, and the chiefs withdrew.</content>
<content>So soon as they had gone Good went
to the little box where he kept his medicines, unlocked it, and took
out a note-book, in the fly-leaves of which was an almanack.</content>
<content>"Now look
here, you fellows, isn't  to-morrow the 4th of June?"</content>
<content>he said.</content>
<content>We had kept a careful note of the days, so were able to answer that it
was.</content>
<content>"Very good; then here we have it - '4 June, total eclipse of the moon
commences at 8.15 Greenwich time, visible in Teneriffe - South Africa ,
 c.' There's a sign for you.</content>
<content>Tell them we will darken the moon
to-morrow night."</content>
<content>The idea was a splendid one; indeed, the only weak spot about it was a
fear lest Good's almanack might be incorrect.</content>
<content>If we made a false
prophecy on such a subject, our prestige would be gone for ever, and so
would Ignosi's chance of the throne of the Kukuanas.</content>
<content>"Suppose that the almanack is wrong," suggested Sir Henry to Good, who
was busily employed in working out something on a blank page of the
book.</content>
<content>"I see no reason to suppose anything of the sort," was his answer.</content>
<content>"Eclipses always come up to time; at least that is my experience of
them, and it especially states that this one will be visible in South
Africa.</content>
<content>I have worked out the reckonings as well as I can, without
knowing our exact position; and I make out that the eclipse should
begin here about ten o'clock tomorrow night, and last till half-past
twelve.</content>
<content>For an hour and a half or so there should be almost total
darkness."</content>
<content>"Well," said Sir Henry, "I suppose we had better risk it."</content>
<content>I acquiesced, though doubtfully, for eclipses are queer cattle to deal
with - it might be a cloudy night, for instance, or our dates might be
wrong - and sent Umbopa to summon the chiefs back.</content>
<content>Presently they came,
and I addressed them thus - 

"Great men of the Kukuanas, and thou, Infadoos, listen.</content>
<content>We love not to
show our powers, for to do so is to interfere with the course of
nature, and to plunge the world into fear and confusion.</content>
<content>But since this
matter is a great one, and as we are angered against the king because
of the slaughter we have seen, and because of the act of the Isanusi 
Gagool, who would have put our friend Ignosi to death, we have
determined to break a rule, and to give such a sign as all men may see.</content>
<content>Come hither"; and I led them to the door of the hut and pointed to the
red ball of the moon.</content>
<content>"What see ye there?"</content>
<content>"We see the sinking moon," answered the spokesman of the party.</content>
<content>"It is so.</content>
<content>Now tell me, can any mortal man put out that moon before her
hour of setting, and bring the curtain of black night down upon the
land?"</content>
<content>The chief laughed a little at the question.</content>
<content>"No, my lord, that no man
can do.</content>
<content>The moon is stronger than man who looks on her, nor can she
vary in her courses."</content>
<content>"Ye say so.</content>
<content>Yet I tell you that to-morrow night, about two hours before
midnight, we will cause the moon to be eaten up for a space of an hour
and half an hour.</content>
<content>Yes, deep darkness shall cover the earth, and it
shall be for a sign that Ignosi is indeed king of the Kukuanas.</content>
<content>If we
do this thing, will ye be satisfied?"</content>
<content>"Yea, my lords," answered the old chief with a smile, which was
reflected on the faces of his companions; " if ye do this thing, we
will be satisfied indeed."</content>
<content>"It shall be done; we three, Incubu, Bougwan, and Macumazahn, have said
it, and it shall be done.</content>
<content>Dost thou hear, Infadoos?"</content>
<content>"I hear, my lord, but it is a wonderful thing that ye promise, to put
out the moon, the mother of the world, when she is at her full."</content>
<content>"Yet shall we do it, Infadoos."</content>
<content>"It is well, my lords.</content>
<content>To-day, two hours after sunset, Twala will send
for my lords to witness the girls dance, and one hour after the dance
begins the girl whom Twala thinks the fairest shall be killed by
Scragga, the king's son, as a sacrifice to the Silent Ones, who sit and
keep watch by the mountains yonder," and he pointed towards the three
strange-looking peaks where Solomon's road was supposed to end.</content>
<content>"Then
let my lords darken the moon, and save the maiden's life, and the
people will believe indeed."</content>
<content>"Ay," said the old chief, still smiling a little, "the people will
believe indeed."</content>
<content>"Two miles from Loo," went on Infadoos, "there is a hill curved like a
new moon, a stronghold, where my regiment, and three other regiments
which these chiefs command, are stationed.</content>
<content>This morning we will make a
plan whereby two or three other regiments may be moved there also.</content>
<content>Then, if in truth my lords can darken the moon, in the darkness I will
take my lords by the hand and lead them out of Loo to this place, where
they shall be safe, and thence we can make war upon Twala the king."</content>
<content>"It is good," said I.</content>
<content>"Let leave us to sleep awhile and to make ready
our magic."</content>
<content>Infadoos rose, and, having saluted us, departed with the chiefs.</content>
<content>"My friends," said Ignosi, so soon as they were gone, "can ye do this
wonderful thing, or were ye speaking empty words to the captains?"</content>
<content>"We believe that we can do it, Umbopa - Ignosi, I mean."</content>
<content>"It is strange," he answered, "and had ye not been Englishmen I would
not have believed it; but I have learned that English 'gentlemen' tell
no lies.</content>
<content>If we live through the matter, be sure that I will repay you."</content>
<content>"Ignosi," said Sir Henry, "promise me one thing."</content>
<content>"I will promise, Incubu, my friend, even before I hear it," answered
the big man with a smile.</content>
<content>"What is it?"</content>
<content>"This: that if ever you come to be king of this people you will do away
with the smelling out of wizards such as we saw last night; and that
the killing of men without trial shall no longer take place in the
land."</content>
<content>Ignosi thought for a moment after I had translated this request, and
then answered - 

"The ways of black people are not as the ways of white men, Incubu, nor
do we value life so highly.</content>
<content>Yet I will promise.</content>
<content>If it be in my power to
hold them back, the witch-finders shall hunt no more, nor shall any man
die the death without trial or judgment."</content>
<content>"That's a bargain, then," said Sir Henry; "and now let us get a little
rest."</content>
<content>Thoroughly wearied out, we were soon sound asleep, and slept till
Ignosi woke us about eleven o'clock.</content>
<content>Then we rose, washed, and ate a
hearty breakfast.</content>
<content>After that we went outside the hut and walked about,
amusing ourselves with examining the structure of the Kukuana huts and
observing the customs of the women.</content>
<content>"I hope that eclipse will come off," said Sir Henry presently.</content>
<content>"If it does not it will soon be all up with us," I answered mournfully;
"for so sure as we are living men some of those chiefs will tell the
whole story to the king, and then there will be another sort of
eclipse, and one that we shall certainly not like."</content>
<content>Returning to the hut we ate some dinner, and passed the rest of the day
in receiving visits of ceremony and curiosity.</content>
<content>At length the sun set,
and we enjoyed a couple of hours of such quiet as our melancholy
forebodings would allow to us.</content>
<content>Finally, about half-past eight, a
messenger came from Twala to bid us to the great annual "dance of
girls" which was about to be celebrated.</content>
<content>Hastily we put on the chain shirts that the king had sent us, and
taking our rifles and ammunition with us, so as to have them handy in
case we had to fly, as suggested by Infadoos, we started boldly enough,
though with inward fear and trembling.</content>
<content>The great space in front of the
king's kraal bore a very different appearance from that which it had
presented on the previous evening.</content>
<content>In place of the grim ranks of
serried warriors were company after company of Kukuana girls, not
over-dressed, so far as clothing went, but each crowned with a wreath
of flowers, and holding a palm leaf in one hand and a white arum lily
in the other.</content>
<content>In the centre of the open moonlit space sat Twala the
king, with old Gagool at his feet, attended by Infadoos, the boy
Scragga, and twelve guards.</content>
<content>There were also present about a score of
chiefs, amongst whom I recognised most of our friends of the night
before.</content>
<content>Twala greeted us with much apparent cordiality, though I saw him fix
his one eye viciously on Umbopa.</content>
<content>"Welcome, white men from the Stars," he said; "this is another sight
from that which your eyes gazed on by the light of last night's moon,
but it is not so good a sight.</content>
<content>Girls are pleasant, and were it not for
such as these," and he pointed round him, "we should none of us be here
this day; but men are better.</content>
<content>Kisses and the tender words of women are
sweet, but the sound of the clashing of the spears of warriors, and the
smell of men's blood, are sweeter far!</content>
<content>Would ye have wives from among
our people, white men?</content>
<content>If so, choose the fairest here, and ye shall
have them, as many as ye will," and he paused for an answer.</content>
<content>As the prospect did not seem to be without attractions for Good, who,
like most sailors, is of a susceptible nature, - being elderly and wise,
foreseeing the endless complications that anything of the sort would
involve, for women bring trouble so surely as the night follows the
day, I put in a hasty answer - 

"Thanks to thee, O king, but we white men wed only with white women
like ourselves.</content>
<content>Your maidens are fair, but they are not for us!"</content>
<content>The king laughed.</content>
<content>"It is well.</content>
<content>In our land there is a proverb which
runs, 'Women's eyes are always bright, whatever the colour,' and
another that says, 'Love her who is present, for be sure she who is
absent is false to thee;' but perhaps these things are not so in the
Stars.</content>
<content>In a land where men are white all things are possible.</content>
<content>So be it,
white men; the girls will not go begging!</content>
<content>Welcome again; and welcome,
too, thou black one; if Gagool here had won her way, thou wouldst have
been stiff and cold by now.</content>
<content>It is lucky for thee that thou too camest
from the Stars; ha!</content>
<content>ha!"</content>
<content>"I can kill thee before thou killest me, O king," was Ignosi's calm
answer, "and thou shalt be stiff before my limbs cease to bend."</content>
<content>Twala started.</content>
<content>"Thou speakest boldly, boy," he replied angrily;
"presume not too far."</content>
<content>"He may well be bold in whose lips are truth.</content>
<content>The truth is a sharp
spear which flies home and misses not.</content>
<content>It is a message from 'the
Stars,' O king."</content>
<content>Twala scowled, and his one eye gleamed fiercely, but he said nothing
more.</content>
<content>"Let the dance begin," he cried, and then the flower-crowned girls
sprang forward in companies, singing a sweet song and waving the
delicate palms and white lilies.</content>
<content>On they danced, looking faint and
spiritual in the soft, sad light of the risen moon; now whirling round
and round, now meeting in mimic warfare, swaying, eddying here and
there, coming forward, falling back in an ordered confusion delightful
to witness.</content>
<content>At last they paused, and a beautiful young woman sprang out
of the ranks and began to pirouette in front of us with a grace and
vigour which would have put most ballet girls to shame.</content>
<content>At length she
retired exhausted, and another took her place, then another and
another, but none of them, either in grace, skill, or personal
attractions, came up to the first.</content>
<content>When the chosen girls had all danced, the king lifted his hand.</content>
<content>"Which deem ye the fairest, white men?"</content>
<content>he asked.</content>
<content>"The first," said I unthinkingly.</content>
<content>Next second I regretted it, for I
remembered that Infadoos had told us that the fairest woman must be
offered up as a sacrifice.</content>
<content>"Then is my mind as your minds, and my eyes as your eyes.</content>
<content>She is the
fairest!</content>
<content>and a sorry thing it is for her, for she must die!"</content>
<content>" Ay, must die! "</content>
<content>piped out Gagool, casting a glance of her quick eyes
in the direction of the poor girl, who, as yet ignorant of the awful
fate in store for her, was standing some ten yards off in front of a
company of maidens, engaged in nervously picking a flower from her
wreath to pieces, petal by petal.</content>
<content>"Why, O king?"</content>
<content>said I, restraining my indignation with difficulty; "the
girl has danced well, and pleased us; she is fair too; it would be hard
to reward her with death."</content>
<content>Twala laughed as he answered - 

"It is our custom, and the figures who sit in stone yonder," and he
pointed towards the three distant peaks, "must have their due.</content>
<content>Did I
fail to put the fairest girl to death to-day, misfortune would fall
upon me and my house.</content>
<content>Thus runs the prophecy of my people: 'If the king
offer not a sacrifice of a fair girl, on the day of the dance of
maidens, to the Old Ones who sit and watch on the mountains, then shall
he fall, and his house.'</content>
<content>Look ye, white men, my brother who reigned
before me offered not the sacrifice, because of the tears of the woman,

<content>It is finished;
she must die!"</content>
<content>Then turning to the guards - "Bring her hither; Scragga,

<content>Two of the men stepped forward, and as they advanced, the girl, for the

<content>But the strong hands caught her fast, and brought her, struggling

<content>"What is thy name, girl?"</content>
<content>piped Gagool.</content>
<content>"What!</content>
<content>wilt thou not answer?</content>
<content>Shall the king's son do his work at once?"</content>
<content>At this hint, Scragga, looking more evil than ever, advanced a step and

<content>The poor girl caught the faint glint of steel through her

<content>She ceased struggling, and clasping

<content>"See," cried Scragga in high glee, 'she shrinks from the sight of my

<content>"If ever I get the chance you shall pay for that, you young hound!"</content>
<content>I
heard Good mutter beneath his breath.</content>
<content>"Now that thou art quiet, give us thy name, my dear.</content>
<content>Come, speak out,
and fear not," said Gagool in mockery.</content>
<content>"Oh, mother," answered the girl, in trembling accents, "my name is
Foulata, of the house of Suko.</content>
<content>Oh, mother, why must I die?</content>
<content>I have done
no wrong!"</content>
<content>"Be comforted," went on the old woman in her hateful tone of mockery.</content>
<content>"Thou must die, indeed, as a sacrifice to the Old Ones who sit yonder,"
and she pointed to the peaks; "but it is better to sleep in the night

<content>The girl Foulata wrung her hands in anguish, and cried out aloud, "Oh,
cruel!</content>
<content>and I so young!</content>
<content>What have I done that I should never again see
the sun rise out of the night, or the stars come following on his track

heavy, or listen to the laughing of the waters?</content>
<content>Woe is me, that I shall
never see my father's hut again, nor feel my mother's kiss, nor tend
the lamb that is sick!</content>
<content>Woe is me, that no lover shall put his arm

<content>Oh, cruel, cruel!"</content>
<content>And again she wrung her hands and turned her tear-stained

<content>Prince Arthur's appeal to the ruffians who came to blind him

<content>But it did not move Gagool or Gagool's master, though I saw signs of

<content>With all a woman's quickness, the

<content>she cried, "throw over me the mantle

<content>"All right, my hearty, I'll look after you," sang out Good in nervous

<content>"Come, get up, there's a good girl," and he stooped and caught

<content>Twala turned and motioned to his son, who advanced with his spear

<content>"Now's your time," whispered Sir Henry to me; "what are you waiting

<content>"I am waiting for that eclipse," I answered; "I have had my eye on the

<content>"Well, you must risk it now, or the girl will be killed.</content>
<content>Twala is
losing patience."</content>
<content>Recognising the force of the argument, and having cast one more

"</content>

<content>I glanced up at the moon despairingly, and now to my intense joy and

<content>On the

<content>Never shall I forget that

<content>Then I lifted my hand solemnly towards the sky, an example which Sir

<content>Sir

<content>Slowly the penumbra, the shadow of a shadow, crept on over the bright

<content>Only

<content>"O Moon!</content>
<content>Moon!</content>
<content>Moon!</content>
<content>wherefore art thou so cold and fickle?"</content>
<content>This appropriate

<content>For ten minutes he went

<content>Meanwhile the dark ring crept on, while all that great assembly fixed
their eyes upon the sky and stared and stared in fascinated silence.</content>
<content>Strange and unholy shadows encroached upon the moonlight, an ominous
quiet filled the place.</content>
<content>Everything grew still as death.</content>
<content>Slowly and in
the midst of this most solemn silence the minutes sped away, and while
they sped the full moon passed deeper and deeper into the shadow of the

the lunar craters.</content>
<content>The great pale orb seemed to draw near and to grow
in size.</content>
<content>She turned a coppery hue, then that portion of her surface
which was unobscured as yet grew grey and ashen, and at length, as

<content>On, yet on, crept the ring of darkness; it was now more than half

<content>The air grew thick, and still more deeply

<content>On, yet on, till we could scarcely see the

<content>No sound rose now from the

<content>"The moon is dying - the white wizards have killed the moon," yelled the

<content>"We shall all perish in the dark," and animated

<content>But he forgot the mail shirts that the

<content>The steel
rebounded harmless, and before he could repeat the blow Curtis had

<content>Scragga dropped dead.</content>
<content>At the sight, and driven mad with fear of the gathering darkness, and

for the gateways.</content>
<content>Nor did the panic stop there.</content>
<content>The king himself,
followed by his guards, some of the chiefs, and Gagool, who hobbled

another minute we ourselves, the would-be victim Foulata, Infadoos, and

<content>"Chiefs," I said, "we have given you the sign.</content>
<content>If ye are satisfied, let
us fly swiftly to the place of which ye spoke.</content>
<content>The charm cannot now be
stopped.</content>
<content>It will work for an hour and the half of an hour.</content>
<content>Let us cover
ourselves in the darkness."</content>
<content>"Come," said Infadoos, turning to go, an example which was followed by

<content>Before we reached the gate of the kraal the moon went out utterly, and
from every quarter of the firmament the stars rushed forth into the
inky sky.</content>
<content>Holding each other by the hand we stumbled on through the darkness.</content>
<content>CHAPTER XII

BEFORE THE BATTLE

Luckily for us, Infadoos and the chiefs knew all the paths of the great
town perfectly, so that we passed by side-ways unmolested, and
notwithstanding the gloom we made fair progress.</content>
<content>For an hour or more we journeyed on, till at length the eclipse began
to pass, and that edge of the moon which had disappeared the first
became again visible.</content>
<content>Suddenly, as we watched, there burst from it a
silver streak of light, accompanied by a wondrous ruddy glow, which
hung upon the blackness of the sky like a celestial lamp, and a wild
and lovely sight it was.</content>
<content>In another five minutes the stars began to
fade, and there was sufficient light to see our whereabouts.</content>
<content>We then
discovered that we were clear of the town of Loo, and approaching a
large flat-topped hill, measuring some two miles in circumference.</content>
<content>This
hill, which is of a formation common in South Africa, is not very high;
indeed, its greatest elevation is scarcely more than 200 feet, but it
is shaped like a horseshoe, and its sides are rather precipitous and
strewn with boulders.</content>
<content>On the grass table-land at its summit is ample
camping-ground, which had been utilised as a military cantonment of no
mean strength.</content>
<content>Its ordinary garrison was one regiment of three thousand
men, but as we toiled up the steep side of the mountain in the
returning moonlight we perceived that there were several of such
regiments encamped there.</content>
<content>Reaching the table-land at last, we found crowds of men roused from
their sleep, shivering with fear and huddled up together in the utmost
consternation at the natural phenomenon which they were witnessing.</content>
<content>Passing through these without a word, we gained a hut in the centre of
the ground, where we were astonished to find two men waiting, laden
with our few goods and chattels, which of course we had been obliged to
leave behind in our hasty flight.</content>
<content>"I sent for them," explained Infadoos; "and also for these," and he
lifted up Good's long-lost trousers.</content>
<content>With an exclamation of rapturous delight Good sprang at them, and
instantly proceeded to put them on.</content>
<content>"Surely my lord will not hide his beautiful white legs!"</content>
<content>exclaimed
Infadoos regretfully.</content>
<content>But Good persisted, and once only did the Kukuana people get the chance
of seeing his beautiful legs again.</content>
<content>Good is a very modest man.</content>
<content>Henceforward they had to satisfy their Ã¦sthetic longings with his one
whisker, his transparent eye, and his movable teeth.</content>
<content>Still gazing with fond remembrance at Good's trousers, Infadoos next
informed us that he had commanded the regiments to muster so soon as
the day broke, in order to explain to them fully the origin and
circumstances of the rebellion which was decided on by the chiefs, and
to introduce to them the rightful heir to the throne, Ignosi.</content>
<content>Accordingly, when the sun was up, the troops - in all some twenty
thousand men, and the flower of the Kukuana army - were mustered on a
large open space, to which we went.</content>
<content>The men were drawn up in three
sides of a dense square, and presented a magnificent spectacle.</content>
<content>We took
our station on the open side of the square, and were speedily
surrounded by all the principal chiefs and officers.</content>
<content>These, after silence had been proclaimed, Infadoos proceeded to
address.</content>
<content>He narrated to them in vigorous and graceful language - for,
like most Kukuanas of high rank, he was a born orator - the history of
Ignosi's father, and of how he had been basely murdered by Twala the
king, and his wife and child driven out to starve.</content>
<content>Then he pointed out
that the people suffered and groaned under Twala's cruel rule,
instancing the proceedings of the previous night, when, under pretence
of their being evil-doers, many of the noblest in the land had been
dragged forth and wickedly done to death.</content>
<content>Next he went on to say that
the white lords from the Stars, looking down upon their country, had
perceived its trouble, and determined, at great personal inconvenience,
to alleviate its lot: That they had accordingly taken the real king of
the Kukuanas, Ignosi, who was languishing in exile, by the hand, and
led him over the mountains: That they had seen the wickedness of
Twala's doings, and for a sign to the wavering, and to save the life of
the girl Foulata, actually, by the exercise of their high magic, had
put out the moon and slain the young fiend Scragga; and that they were
prepared to stand by them, and assist them to overthrow Twala, and set
up the rightful king, Ignosi, in his place.</content>
<content>He finished his discourse amidst a murmur of approbation.</content>
<content>Then Ignosi
stepped forward and began to speak.</content>
<content>Having reiterated all that Infadoos
his uncle had said, he concluded a powerful speech in these words: - 

"O chiefs, captains, soldiers, and people, ye have heard my words.</content>
<content>Now
must ye make choice between me and him who sits upon my throne, the
uncle who killed his brother, and hunted his brother's child forth to
die in the cold and the night.</content>
<content>That I am indeed the king
these" - pointing to the chiefs - "can tell you, for they have seen the
snake about my middle.</content>
<content>If I were not the king, would these white men be
on my side with all their magic?</content>
<content>Tremble, chiefs, captains, soldiers,
and people!</content>
<content>Is not the darkness they have brought upon the land to
confound Twala and cover our flight, darkness even in the hour of the
full moon, yet before your eyes?"</content>
<content>"It is," answered the soldiers.</content>
<content>"I am the king; I say to you, I am the king," went on Ignosi, drawing
up his great stature to its full, and lifting his broad-bladed
battle-axe above his head.</content>
<content>"If there be any man among you who says that
it is not so, let him stand forth and I will fight him now, and his
blood shall be a red token that I tell you true.</content>
<content>Let him stand forth, I
say;" and he shook the great axe till it flashed in the sunlight.</content>
<content>As nobody seemed inclined to respond to this heroic version of "Dilly,
Dilly, come and be killed," our late henchman proceeded with his
address.</content>
<content>"I am indeed the king, and should ye stand by my side in the battle, if
I win the day ye shall go with me to victory and honour.</content>
<content>I will give
you oxen and wives, and ye shall take place of all the regiments; and
if ye fall, I will fall with you.</content>
<content>"And behold, I give you this promise, that when I sit upon the seat of
my fathers, bloodshed shall cease in the land.</content>
<content>No longer shall ye cry
for justice to find slaughter, no longer shall the witch-finder hunt
you out so that ye may be slain without a cause.</content>
<content>No man shall die save
he who offends against the laws.</content>
<content>The 'eating up' of your kraals shall
cease; each one of you shall sleep secure in his own hut and fear
naught, and justice shall walk blindfold throughout the land.</content>
<content>Have ye
chosen, chiefs, captains, soldiers, and people?"</content>
<content>"We have chosen, O king," came back the answer.</content>
<content>"It is well.</content>
<content>Turn your heads and see how Twala's messengers go forth
from the great town, east and west, and north and south, to gather a
mighty army to slay me and you, and these my friends and protectors.</content>
<content>To-morrow, or perchance the next day, he will come against us with all
who are faithful to him.</content>
<content>Then I shall see the man who is indeed my man,
the man who fears not to die for his cause; and I tell you that he
shall not be forgotten in the time of spoil.</content>
<content>I have spoken, O chiefs,
captains, soldiers, and people.</content>
<content>Now go to your huts and make you ready
for war."</content>
<content>There was a pause, till presently one of the chiefs lifted his hand,
and out rolled the royal salute, " Koom. "</content>
<content>It was a sign that the
soldiers accepted Ignosi as their king.</content>
<content>Then they marched off in
battalions.</content>
<content>Half an hour afterwards we held a council of war, at which all the
commanders of regiments were present.</content>
<content>It was evident to us that before
very long we should be attacked in overwhelming force.</content>
<content>Indeed, from our
point of vantage on the hill we could see troops mustering, and runners
going forth from Loo in every direction, doubtless to summon soldiers
to the king's assistance.</content>
<content>We had on our side about twenty thousand men,
composed of seven of the best regiments in the country.</content>
<content>Twala, so
Infadoos and the chiefs calculated, had at least thirty to thirty-five
thousand on whom he could rely at present assembled in Loo, and they
thought that by midday on the morrow he would be able to gather another
five thousand or more to his aid.</content>
<content>It was, of course, possible that some
of his troops would desert and come over to us, but it was not a
contingency which could be reckoned on.</content>
<content>Meanwhile, it was clear that
active preparations were being made by Twala to subdue us.</content>
<content>Already
strong bodies of armed men were patrolling round and round the foot of
the hill, and there were other signs also of coming assault.</content>
<content>Infadoos and the chiefs, however, were of opinion that no attack would
take place that day, which would be devoted to preparation and to the
removal of every available means of the moral effect produced upon the
minds of the soldiery by the supposed magical darkening of the moon.</content>
<content>The onslaught would be on the morrow, they said, and they proved to be
right.</content>
<content>Meanwhile, we set to work to strengthen the position in all ways
possible.</content>
<content>Almost every man was turned out, and in the course of the
day, which seemed far too short, much was done.</content>
<content>The paths up the
hill - that was rather a sanatorium than a fortress, being used
generally as the camping place of regiments suffering from recent
service in unhealthy portions of the country - were carefully blocked
with masses of stones, and every other approach was made as impregnable
as time would allow.</content>
<content>Piles of boulders were collected at various spots
to be rolled down upon an advancing enemy, stations were appointed to
the different regiments, and all preparation was made which our joint
ingenuity could suggest.</content>
<content>Just before sundown, as we rested after our toil, we perceived a small
company of men advancing towards us from the direction of Loo, one of
whom bore a palm leaf in his hand for a sign that he came as a herald.</content>
<content>As he drew near, Ignosi, Infadoos, one or two chiefs and ourselves,
went down to the foot of the mountain to meet him.</content>
<content>He was a
gallant-looking fellow, wearing the regulation leopard-skin cloak.</content>
<content>"Greeting!"</content>
<content>he cried, as he came; "the king's greeting to those who
make unholy war against the king; the lion's greeting to the jackals
that snarl around his heels."</content>
<content>"Speak," I said.</content>
<content>"These are the king's words.</content>
<content>Surrender to the king's mercy ere a worse
thing befall you.</content>
<content>Already the shoulder has been torn from the black
bull, and the king drives him bleeding about the camp."</content>
<content>1 

"What are Twala's terms?"</content>
<content>I asked from curiosity.</content>
<content>"His terms are merciful, worthy of a great king.</content>
<content>These are the words of
Twala, the one-eyed, the mighty, the husband of a thousand wives, lord
of the Kukuanas, keeper of the Great Road  Solomon's Road , beloved of
the Strange Ones who sit in silence at the mountains yonder  the Three
Witches , Calf of the Black Cow, Elephant whose tread shakes the earth,
Terror of the evil-doer, Ostrich whose feet devour the desert, huge
One, black One, wise One, king from generation to generation!</content>
<content>these are
the words of Twala: 'I will have mercy and be satisfied with a little
blood.</content>
<content>One in every ten shall die, the rest shall go free; but the
white man Incubu, who slew Scragga my son, and the black man his
servant, who pretends to my throne, and Infadoos my brother, who brews
rebellion against me, these shall die by torture as an offering to the
Silent Ones.'</content>
<content>Such are the merciful words of Twala."</content>
<content>After consulting with the others a little, I answered him in a loud
voice, so that the soldiers might hear, thus - 

"Go back, thou dog, to Twala, who sent thee, and say that we, Ignosi,
veritable king of the Kukuanas, Incubu, Bougwan, and Macumazahn, the
wise ones from the Stars, who make dark the moon, Infadoos, of the
royal house, and the chiefs, captains, and people here gathered, make
answer and say, 'That we will not surrender; that before the sun has
gone down twice, Twala's corpse shall stiffen at Twala's gate, and
Ignosi, whose father Twala slew, shall reign in his stead.'</content>
<content>Now go, ere
we whip thee away, and beware how thou dost lift a hand against such as
we are."</content>
<content>The herald laughed loudly.</content>
<content>"Ye frighten not men with such swelling
words," he cried out.</content>
<content>"Show yourselves as bold to-morrow, O ye who
darken the moon.</content>
<content>Be bold, fight, and be merry, before the crows pick
your bones till they are whiter than your faces.</content>
<content>Farewell; perhaps we
may meet in the fight; fly not to the Stars, but wait for me, I pray,
white men."</content>
<content>With this shaft of sarcasm he retired, and almost
immediately the sun sank.</content>
<content>That night was a busy one, for weary as we were, so far as was possible
by the moonlight all preparations for the morrow's fight were
continued, and messengers were constantly coming and going from the
place where we sat in council.</content>
<content>At last, about an hour after midnight,
everything that could be done was done, and the camp, save for the
occasional challenge of a sentry, sank into silence.</content>
<content>Sir Henry and I,
accompanied by Ignosi and one of the chiefs, descended the hill and
made a round of the pickets.</content>
<content>As we went, suddenly, from all sorts of
unexpected places, spears gleamed out in the moonlight, only to vanish
again when we uttered the password.</content>
<content>It was clear to us that none were
sleeping at their posts.</content>
<content>Then we returned, picking our way warily
through thousands of sleeping warriors, many of whom were taking their
last earthly rest.</content>
<content>The moonlight flickering along their spears, played upon their features
and made them ghastly; the chilly night wind tossed their tall and
hearse-like plumes.</content>
<content>There they lay in wild confusion, with arms
outstretched and twisted limbs; their stern, stalwart forms looking
weird and unhuman in the moonlight.</content>
<content>"How many of these do you suppose will be alive at this time
to-morrow?"</content>
<content>asked Sir Henry.</content>
<content>I shook my head and looked again at the sleeping men, and to my tired
and yet excited imagination it seemed as though Death had already
touched them.</content>
<content>My mind's eye singled out those who were sealed to
slaughter, and there rushed in upon my heart a great sense of the
mystery of human life, and an overwhelming sorrow at its futility and
sadness.</content>
<content>To-night these thousands slept their healthy sleep, to-morrow
they, and many others with them, ourselves perhaps among them, would be
stiffening in the cold; their wives would be widows, their children
fatherless, and their place know them no more for ever.</content>
<content>Only the old
moon would shine on serenely, the night wind would stir the grasses,
and the wide earth would take its rest, even as it did Ã¦ons before we
were, and will do Ã¦ons after we have been forgotten.</content>
<content>Yet man dies not whilst the world, at once his mother and his monument,
remains.</content>
<content>His name is lost, indeed, but the breath he breathed still
stirs the pine-tops on the mountains, the sound of the words he spoke
yet echoes on through space; the thoughts his brain gave birth to we
have inherited to-day; his passions are our cause of life; the joys and
sorrows that he knew are our familiar friends - the end from which he
fled aghast will surely overtake us also!</content>
<content>Truly the universe is full of ghosts, not sheeted churchyard spectres,
but the inextinguishable elements of individual life, which having once
been, can never die , though they blend and change, and change again
for ever.</content>
<content>All sorts of reflections of this nature passed through my mind - for as
I grow older I regret to say that a detestable habit of thinking seems
to be getting a hold of me - while I stood and stared at those grim yet
fantastic lines of warriors, sleeping, as their saying goes, "upon
their spears."</content>
<content>"Curtis," I said, "I am in a condition of pitiable fear."</content>
<content>Sir Henry stroked his yellow beard and laughed, as he answered - 

"I have heard you make that sort of remark before, Quatermain."</content>
<content>"Well, I mean it now.</content>
<content>Do you know, I very much doubt if one of us will
be alive to-morrow night.</content>
<content>We shall be attacked in overwhelming force,
and it is quite a chance if we can hold this place."</content>
<content>"We'll give a good account of some of them, at any rate.</content>
<content>Look here,
Quatermain, this business is nasty, and one with which, properly
speaking, we ought not to be mixed up, but we are in for it, so we must
make the best of our job.</content>
<content>Speaking personally, I had rather be killed
fighting than any other way, and now that there seems little chance of
our finding my poor brother, it makes the idea easier to me.</content>
<content>But
fortune favours the brave, and we may succeed.</content>
<content>Anyway, the battle will
be awful, and having a reputation to keep up, we shall need to be in
the thick of the thing."</content>
<content>He made this last remark in a mournful voice, but there was a gleam in
his eye which belied its melancholy.</content>
<content>I have an idea Sir Henry Curtis
actually likes fighting.</content>
<content>After this we went to sleep for a couple of hours or so.</content>
<content>Just about dawn we were awakened by Infadoos, who came to say that
great activity was to be observed in Loo, and that parties of the
king's skirmishers were driving in our outposts.</content>
<content>We rose and dressed ourselves for the fray, each putting on his chain
armour shirt, for which garments at the present juncture we felt
exceedingly thankful.</content>
<content>Sir Henry went the whole length about the matter,
and dressed himself like a native warrior.</content>
<content>"When you are in
Kukuanaland, do as the Kukuanas do," he remarked, as he drew the
shining steel over his broad breast, which it fitted like a glove.</content>
<content>Nor
did he stop there.</content>
<content>At his request Infadoos had provided him with a
complete set of native war uniform.</content>
<content>Round his throat he fastened the
leopard-skin cloak of a commanding officer, on his brows he bound the
plume of black ostrich feathers worn only by generals of high rank, and
about his middle a magnificent moocha of white ox-tails.</content>
<content>A pair of
sandals, a leglet of goat's hair, a heavy battle-axe with a
rhinoceros-horn handle, a round iron shield covered with white ox-hide,
and the regulation number of tollas , or throwing-knives, made up his
equipment, to which, however, he added his revolver.</content>
<content>The dress was, no
doubt, a savage one, but I am bound to say that I seldom saw a finer
sight than Sir Henry Curtis presented in this guise.</content>
<content>It showed off his
magnificent physique to the greatest advantage, and when Ignosi arrived
presently, arrayed in a similar costume, I thought to myself that I had
never before seen two such splendid men.</content>
<content>As for Good and myself, the armour did not suit us nearly so well.</content>
<content>To
begin with, Good insisted upon keeping on his new-found trousers, and a
stout, short gentleman with an eye-glass, and one half of his face
shaved, arrayed in a mail shirt, carefully tucked into a very seedy
pair of corduroys, looks more remarkable than imposing.</content>
<content>In my case, the
chain shirt being too big for me, I put it on over all my clothes,
which caused it to bulge in a somewhat ungainly fashion.</content>
<content>I discarded my
trousers, however, retaining only my veldtschoons, having determined to
go into battle with bare legs, in order to be the lighter for running,
in case it became necessary to retire quickly.</content>
<content>The mail coat, a spear,
a shield, that I did not know how to use, a couple of tollas , a
revolver, and a huge plume, which I pinned into the top of my shooting
hat, in order to give a bloodthirsty finish to my appearance, completed
my modest equipment.</content>
<content>In addition to all these articles, of course we
had our rifles, but as ammunition was scarce, and as they would be
useless in case of a charge, we arranged that they should be carried
behind us by bearers.</content>
<content>When at length we had equipped ourselves, we swallowed some food
hastily, and then started out to see how things were going on.</content>
<content>At one
point in the table-land of the mountain, there was a little koppie of
brown stone, which served the double purpose of head-quarters and of a
conning tower.</content>
<content>Here we found Infadoos surrounded by his own regiment,
the Greys, which was undoubtedly the finest in the Kukuana army, and
the same that we had first seen at the outlying kraal.</content>
<content>This regiment,
now three thousand five hundred strong, was being held in reserve, and
the men were lying down on the grass in companies, and watching the
king's forces creep out of Loo in long ant-like columns.</content>
<content>There seemed
to be no end to the length of these columns - three in all, and each of
them numbering, as we judged, at least eleven or twelve thousand men.</content>
<content>As soon as they were clear of the town the regiments formed up.</content>
<content>Then
one body marched off to the right, one to the left, and the third came
on slowly towards us.</content>
<content>"Ah," said Infadoos, "they are going to attack us on three sides at
once."</content>
<content>This seemed rather serious news, for our position on the top of the
mountain, which measured a mile and a half in circumference, being an
extended one, it was important to us to concentrate our comparatively
small defending force as much as possible.</content>
<content>But since it was impossible
for us to dictate in what way we should be assailed, we had to make the
best of it, and accordingly sent orders to the various regiments to
prepare to receive the separate onslaughts.</content>
<content>1  This cruel custom is not confined to the Kukuanas, but is by no
means uncommon amongst African tribes on the occasion of the outbreak
of war or any other important public event.</content>
<content>- A.Q.</content>
<content>CHAPTER XIII

THE ATTACK

Slowly, and without the slightest appearance of haste or excitement,
the three columns crept on.</content>
<content>When within about five hundred yards of us,
the main or centre column halted at the root of a tongue of open plain
which ran up into the hill, to give time to the other divisions to
circumvent our position, which was shaped more or less in the form of a
horse-shoe, with its two points facing towards the town of Loo.</content>
<content>The
object of this manoeuvre was that the threefold assault should be
delivered simultaneously.</content>
<content>"Oh, for a gatling!"</content>
<content>groaned Good, as he contemplated the serried
phalanxes beneath us.</content>
<content>"I would clear that plain in twenty minutes."</content>
<content>"We have not got one, so it is no use yearning for it; but suppose you
try a shot, Quatermain," said Sir Henry.</content>
<content>"See how near you can go to
that tall fellow who appears to be in command.</content>
<content>Two to one you miss him,
and an even sovereign, to be honestly paid if ever we get out of this,
that you don't  drop the bullet within five yards."</content>
<content>This piqued me, so, loading the express with solid ball, I waited till
my friend walked some ten yards out from his force, in order to get a
better view of our position, accompanied only by an orderly; then,
lying down and resting the express on a rock, I covered him.</content>
<content>The rifle,
like all expresses, was only sighted to three hundred and fifty yards,
so to allow for the drop in trajectory I took him half-way down the
neck, which ought, I calculated, to find him in the chest.</content>
<content>He stood
quite still and gave me every opportunity, but whether it was the
excitement or the wind, or the fact of the man being a long shot, I
don't  know, but this was what happened.</content>
<content>Getting dead on, as I thought,
a fine sight, I pressed, and when the puff of smoke had cleared away,
to my disgust, I saw my man standing there unharmed, whilst his
orderly, who was at least three paces to the left, was stretched upon
the ground apparently dead.</content>
<content>Turning swiftly, the officer I had aimed at
began to run towards his men in evident alarm.</content>
<content>"Bravo, Quatermain!"</content>
<content>sang out Good; "you've  frightened him."</content>
<content>This made me very angry, for, if possible to avoid it, I hate to miss
in public.</content>
<content>When a man is master of only one art he likes to keep up his
reputation in that art.</content>
<content>Moved quite out of myself at my failure, I did
a rash thing.</content>
<content>Rapidly covering the general as he ran, I let drive with
the second barrel.</content>
<content>Instantly the poor man threw up his arms, and fell
forward on to his face.</content>
<content>This time I had made no mistake; and - I say it
as a proof of how little we think of others when our own safety, pride,
or reputation is in question - I was brute enough to feel delighted at
the sight.</content>
<content>The regiments who had seen the feat cheered wildly at this exhibition
of the white man's magic, which they took as an omen of success, while
the force the general had belonged to - which, indeed, as we ascertained
afterwards, he had commanded - fell back in confusion.</content>
<content>Sir Henry and
Good now took up their rifles and began to fire, the latter
industriously "browning" the dense mass before him with another
Winchester repeater, and I also had another shot or two, with the
result, so far as we could judge, that we put some six or eight men
 hors de combat before they were out of range.</content>
<content>Just as we stopped firing there came an ominous roar from our far
right, then a similar roar rose on our left.</content>
<content>The two other divisions
were engaging us.</content>
<content>At the sound, the mass of men before us opened out a little, and
advanced towards the hill and up the spit of bare grass land at a slow
trot, singing a deep-throated song as they ran.</content>
<content>We kept up a steady
fire from our rifles as they came, Ignosi joining in occasionally, and
accounted for several men, but of course we produced no more effect
upon that mighty rush of armed humanity than he who throws pebbles does
on the breaking wave.</content>
<content>On they came, with a shout and the clashing of spears; now they were
driving in the pickets we had placed among the rocks at the foot of the
hill.</content>
<content>After that the advance was a little slower, for though as yet we
had offered no serious opposition, the attacking forces must climb up
hill, and they came slowly to save their breath.</content>
<content>Our first line of
defence was about half-way down the side of the slope, our second fifty
yards further back, while our third occupied the edge of the plateau.</content>
<content>On they stormed, shouting their war-cry, " Twala!</content>
<content>Twala!</content>
<content>Chiele!</content>
<content>Chiele! "</content>
<content>Twala!</content>
<content>Twala!</content>
<content>Smite!</content>
<content>Smite!  "</content>
<content>Ignosi!</content>
<content>Ignosi!</content>
<content>Chiele!</content>
<content>Chiele! "</content>
<content>answered our people.</content>
<content>They were quite close now, and the
 tollas , or throwing-knives, began to flash backwards and forwards,
and now with an awful yell the battle closed in.</content>
<content>To and fro swayed the mass of struggling warriors, men falling fast as
leaves in an autumn wind; but before long the superior weight of the
attacking force began to tell, and our first line of defence was slowly
pressed back till it merged into the second.</content>
<content>Here the struggle was very
fierce, but again our people were driven back and up, till at length,
within twenty minutes of the commencement of the fight, our third line
came into action.</content>
<content>But by this time the assailants were much exhausted, and besides had
lost many men killed and wounded, and to break through that third
impenetrable hedge of spears proved beyond their powers.</content>
<content>For a while
the seething lines of savages swung backwards and forwards, in the
fierce ebb and flow of battle, and the issue was doubtful.</content>
<content>Sir Henry
watched the desperate struggle with a kindling eye, and then without a
word he rushed off, followed by Good, and flung himself into the
hottest of the fray.</content>
<content>As for myself, I stopped where I was.</content>
<content>The soldiers caught sight of his tall form as he plunged into battle,
and there rose a cry of - 

" Nanzia Incubu!</content>
<content>Nanzia Unkungunklovo! "</content>
<content>Here is the Elephant! 
"</content>
<content>Chiele!</content>
<content>Chiele! "</content>
<content>From that moment the end was no longer in doubt.</content>
<content>Inch by inch, fighting
with splendid gallantry, the attacking force was pressed back down the
hillside, till at last it retreated upon its reserves in something like
confusion.</content>
<content>At that instant, too, a messenger arrived to say that the
left attack had been repulsed; and I was just beginning to congratulate
myself, believing that the affair was over for the present, when, to
our horror, we perceived our men who had been engaged in the right
defence being driven towards us across the plain, followed by swarms of
the enemy, who had evidently succeeded at this point.</content>
<content>Ignosi, who was standing by me, took in the situation at a glance, and
issued a rapid order.</content>
<content>Instantly the reserve regiment around us, the
Greys, extended itself.</content>
<content>Again Ignosi gave a word of command, which was taken up and repeated by
the captains, and in another second, to my intense disgust, I found
myself involved in a furious onslaught upon the advancing foe.</content>
<content>Getting
as much as I could behind Ignosi's huge frame, I made the best of a bad
job, and toddled along to be killed as though I liked it.</content>
<content>In a minute
or two - we were plunging through the flying groups of our men, who at
once began to re-form behind us, and then I am sure I do not know what
happened.</content>
<content>All I can remember is a dreadful rolling noise of the meeting
of shields, and the sudden apparition of a huge ruffian, whose eyes
seemed literally to be starting out of his head, making straight at me
with a bloody spear.</content>
<content>But - I say it with pride - I rose - or rather
sank - to the occasion.</content>
<content>It was one before which most people would have
collapsed once and for all.</content>
<content>Seeing that if I stood where I was I must
be killed, as the horrid apparition came I flung myself down in front
of him so cleverly that, being unable to stop himself, he took a header
right over my prostrate form.</content>
<content>Before he could rise again, I had risen
and settled the matter from behind with my revolver.</content>
<content>Shortly after this somebody knocked me down, and I remember no more of
that charge.</content>
<content>When I came to I found myself back at the koppie, with Good bending
over me holding some water in a gourd.</content>
<content>"How do you feel, old fellow?"</content>
<content>he asked anxiously.</content>
<content>I got up and shook myself before replying.</content>
<content>"Pretty well, thank you," I answered.</content>
<content>"Thank Heaven!</content>
<content>When I saw them carry you in, I felt quite sick; I
thought you were done for."</content>
<content>"Not this time, my boy.</content>
<content>I fancy I only got a rap on the head, which
knocked me stupid.</content>
<content>How has it ended?"</content>
<content>"They are repulsed at every point for a while.</content>
<content>The loss is dreadfully
heavy; we have quite two thousand killed and wounded, and they must
have lost three.</content>
<content>Look, there's a sight!"</content>
<content>and he pointed to long lines
of men advancing by fours.</content>
<content>In the centre of every group of four, and being borne by it, was a kind
of hide tray, of which a Kukuana force always carries a quantity, with
a loop for a handle at each corner.</content>
<content>On these trays - and their number
seemed endless - lay wounded men, who as they arrived were hastily
examined by the medicine men, of whom ten were attached to a regiment.</content>
<content>If the wound was not of a fatal character the sufferer was taken away
and attended to as carefully as circumstances would allow.</content>
<content>But if, on
the other hand, the injured man's condition proved hopeless, what
followed was very dreadful, though doubtless it may have been the
truest mercy.</content>
<content>One of the doctors, under pretence of carrying out an
examination, swiftly opened an artery with a sharp knife, and in a
minute or two the sufferer expired painlessly.</content>
<content>There were many cases
that day in which this was done.</content>
<content>In fact, it was done in the majority
of cases when the wound was in the body, for the gash made by the entry
of the enormously broad spears used by the Kukuanas generally rendered
recovery impossible.</content>
<content>In most instances the poor sufferers were already
unconscious, and in others the fatal "nick" of the artery was inflicted
so swiftly and painlessly that they did not seem to notice it.</content>
<content>Still it
was a ghastly sight, and one from which we were glad to escape; indeed,
I never remember anything of the kind that affected me more than seeing
those gallant soldiers thus put out of pain by the red-handed medicine
men, except, indeed, on one occasion when, after an attack, I saw a
force of Swazis burying their hopelessly wounded alive .</content>
<content>Hurrying from this dreadful scene to the further side of the koppie, we
found Sir Henry, who still held a battle-axe in his hand, Ignosi,
Infadoos, and one or two of the chiefs in deep consultation.</content>
<content>"Thank Heaven, here you are, Quatermain!</content>
<content>I can't  quite make out what
Ignosi wants to do.</content>
<content>It seems that though we have beaten off the attack,
Twala is now receiving large reinforcements, and is showing a
disposition to invest us, with the view of starving us out."</content>
<content>"That's awkward."</content>
<content>"Yes; especially as Infadoos says that the water supply has given out."</content>
<content>"My lord, that is so," said Infadoos; "the spring cannot supply the
wants of so great a multitude, and it is failing rapidly.</content>
<content>Before night
we shall all be thirsty.</content>
<content>Listen, Macumazahn.</content>
<content>Thou art wise, and hast
doubtless seen many wars in the lands from whence thou camest - that is
if indeed they make wars in the Stars.</content>
<content>Now tell us, what shall we do?</content>
<content>Twala has brought up many fresh men to take the place of those who have
fallen.</content>
<content>Yet Twala has learnt his lesson; the hawk did not think to find
the heron ready; but our beak has pierced his breast; he fears to
strike at us again.</content>
<content>We too are wounded, and he will wait for us to die;
he will wind himself round us like a snake round a buck, and fight the
fight of 'sit down.'"</content>
<content>"I hear thee," I said.</content>
<content>"So, Macumazahn, thou seest we have no water here, and but a little
food, and we must choose between these three things - to languish like a
starving lion in his den, or to strive to break away towards the north,
or" - and here he rose and pointed towards the dense mass of our
foes - "to launch ourselves straight at Twala's throat.</content>
<content>Incubu, the
great warrior - for to-day he fought like a buffalo in a net, and
Twala's soldiers went down before his axe like young corn before the
hail; with these eyes I saw it - Incubu says 'Charge'; but the Elephant
is ever prone to charge.</content>
<content>Now what says Macumazahn, the wily old fox,
who has seen much, and loves to bite his enemy from behind?</content>
<content>The last
word is in Ignosi the king, for it is a king's right to speak of war;
but let us hear thy voice, O Macumazahn, who watchest by night, and the
voice too of him of the transparent eye."</content>
<content>"What sayest thou, Ignosi," I asked.</content>
<content>"Nay, my father," answered our quondam servant, who now, clad as he was
in the full panoply of savage war, looked every inch a warrior king,
"do thou speak, and let me, who am but a child in wisdom beside thee,
hearken to thy words."</content>
<content>Thus adjured, after taking hasty counsel with Good and Sir Henry, I
delivered my opinion briefly to the effect that, being trapped, our
best chance, especially in view of the failure of our water supply, was
to initiate an attack upon Twala's forces.</content>
<content>Then I recommended that the
attack should be delivered at once, "before our wounds grew stiff," and
also before the sight of Twala's overpowering force caused the hearts
of our soldiers "to wax small like fat before a fire."</content>
<content>Otherwise, I
pointed out, some of the captains might change their minds, and, making
peace with Twala, desert to him, or even betray us into his hands.</content>
<content>This expression of opinion seemed, on the whole, to be favourably
received; indeed, among the Kukuanas my utterances met with a respect
which has never been accorded to them before or since.</content>
<content>But the real
decision as to our plans lay with Ignosi, who, since he had been
recognised as rightful king, could exercise the almost unbounded rights
of sovereignty, including, of course, the final decision on matters of
generalship, and it was to him that all eyes were now turned.</content>
<content>At length, after a pause, during which he appeared to be thinking
deeply, he spoke.</content>
<content>"Incubu, Macumazahn, and Bougwan, brave white men, and my friends;
Infadoos, my uncle, and chiefs; my heart is fixed.</content>
<content>I will strike at
Twala this day, and set my fortunes on the blow, ay, and my life - my
life and your lives also.</content>
<content>Listen; thus will I strike.</content>
<content>Ye see how the
hill curves round like the half-moon, and how the plain runs like a
green tongue towards us within the curve?"</content>
<content>"We see," I answered.</content>
<content>"Good; it is now mid-day, and the men eat and rest after the toil of
battle.</content>
<content>When the sun has turned and travelled a little way towards the
darkness, let thy regiment, my uncle, advance with one other down to
the green tongue, and it shall be that when Twala sees it he will hurl
his force at it to crush it.</content>
<content>But the spot is narrow, and the regiments
can come against thee one at a time only; so may they be destroyed one
by one, and the eyes of all Twala's army shall be fixed upon a struggle
the like of which has not been seen by living man.</content>
<content>And with thee, my
uncle, shall go Incubu my friend, that when Twala sees his battle-axe
flashing in the first rank of the Greys his heart may grow faint.</content>
<content>And I
will come with the second regiment, that which follows thee, so that if
ye are destroyed, as it might happen, there may yet be a king left to
fight for; and with me shall come Macumazahn the wise."</content>
<content>"It is well, O king," said Infadoos, apparently contemplating the
certainty of the complete annihilation of his regiment with perfect
calmness.</content>
<content>Truly, these Kukuanas are a wonderful people.</content>
<content>Death has no
terrors for them when it is incurred in the course of duty.</content>
<content>"And whilst the eyes of the multitude of Twala's soldiers are thus
fixed upon the fight," went on Ignosi, "behold, one-third of the men
who are left alive to us  i.e.</content>
<content>about 6,000  shall creep along the right
horn of the hill and fall upon the left flank of Twala's force, and
one-third shall creep along the left horn and fall upon Twala's right
flank.</content>
<content>And when I see that the horns are ready to toss Twala, then will
I, with the men who remain to me, charge home in Twala's face, and if
fortune goes with us the day will be ours, and before Night drives her
black oxen from the mountains to the mountains we shall sit in peace at
Loo.</content>
<content>And now let us eat and make ready; and, Infadoos, do thou prepare,
that the plan be carried out without fail; and stay, let my white
father Bougwan go with the right horn, that his shining eye may give
courage to the captains."</content>
<content>The arrangements for attack thus briefly indicated were set in motion
with a rapidity that spoke well for the perfection of the Kukuana
military system.</content>
<content>Within little more than an hour rations had been
served out and devoured, the divisions were formed, the scheme of
onslaught was explained to the leaders, and the whole force, numbering
about 18,000 men, was ready to move, with the exception of a guard left
in charge of the wounded.</content>
<content>Presently Good came up to Sir Henry and myself.</content>
<content>"Good-bye, you fellows," he said; "I am off with the right wing
according to orders; and so I have come to shake hands, in case we
should not meet again, you know," he added significantly.</content>
<content>We shook hands in silence, and not without the exhibition of as much
emotion as Anglo-Saxons are wont to show.</content>
<content>"It is a queer business," said Sir Henry, his deep voice shaking a
little, "and I confess I never expect to see to-morrow's sun.</content>
<content>So far as
I can make out, the Greys, with whom I am to go, are to fight until
they are wiped out in order to enable the wings to slip round unawares
and outflank Twala.</content>
<content>Well, so be it; at any rate, it will be a man's
death.</content>
<content>Good-bye, old fellow.</content>
<content>God bless you!</content>
<content>I hope you will pull
through and live to collar the diamonds; but if you do, take my advice
and don't  have anything more to do with Pretenders!"</content>
<content>In another second Good had wrung us both by the hand and gone; and then
Infadoos came up and led off Sir Henry to his place in the forefront of
the Greys, whilst, with many misgivings, I departed with Ignosi to my
station in the second attacking regiment.</content>
<content>CHAPTER XIV

THE LAST STAND OF THE GREYS

In a few more minutes the regiments destined to carry out the flanking
movements had tramped off in silence, keeping carefully to the lee of
the rising ground in order to conceal their advance from the keen eyes
of Twala's scouts.</content>
<content>Half an hour or more was allowed to elapse between the setting out of
the horns or wings of the army before any stir was made by the Greys
and their supporting regiment, known as the Buffaloes, which formed its
chest, and were destined to bear the brunt of the battle.</content>
<content>Both of these regiments were almost perfectly fresh, and of full
strength, the Greys having been in reserve in the morning, and having
lost but a small number of men in sweeping back that part of the attack
which had proved successful in breaking the line of defence, on the
occasion when I charged with them and was stunned for my pains.</content>
<content>As for
the Buffaloes, they had formed the third line of defence on the left,
and since the attacking force at that point had not succeeded in
breaking through the second, they had scarcely come into action at all.</content>
<content>Infadoos, who was a wary old general, and knew the absolute importance
of keeping up the spirits of his men on the eve of such a desperate
encounter, employed the pause in addressing his own regiment, the
Greys, in poetical language: explaining to them the honour that they
were receiving in being put thus in the forefront of the battle, and in
having the great white warrior from the Stars to fight with them in
their ranks; and promising large rewards of cattle and promotion to all
who survived in the event of Ignosi's arms being successful.</content>
<content>I looked down the long lines of waving black plumes and stern faces
beneath them, and sighed to think that within one short hour most, if
not all, of those magnificent veteran warriors, not a man of whom was
under forty years of age, would be laid dead or dying in the dust.</content>
<content>It
could not be otherwise; they were being condemned, with that wise
recklessness of human life which marks the great general, and often
saves his forces and attains his ends, to certain slaughter, in order
to give their cause and the remainder of the army a chance of success.</content>
<content>They were foredoomed to die, and they knew the truth.</content>
<content>It was to be
their task to engage regiment after regiment of Twala's army on the
narrow strip of green beneath us, till they were exterminated or till
the wings found a favourable opportunity for their onslaught.</content>
<content>And yet
they never hesitated, nor could I detect a sign of fear upon the face
of a single warrior.</content>
<content>There they were - going to certain death, about to
quit the blessed light of day for ever, and yet able to contemplate
their doom without a tremor.</content>
<content>Even at that moment I could not help
contrasting their state of mind with my own, which was far from
comfortable, and breathing a sigh of envy and admiration.</content>
<content>Never before
had I seen such an absolute devotion to the idea of duty, and such a
complete indifference to its bitter fruits.</content>
<content>"Behold your king!"</content>
<content>ended old Infadoos, pointing to Ignosi; "go fight
and fall for him, as is the duty of brave men, and cursed and shameful
for ever be the name of him who shrinks from death for his king, or who
turns his back to the foe.</content>
<content>Behold your king, chiefs, captains, and
soldiers!</content>
<content>Now do your homage to the sacred Snake, and then follow on,
that Incubu and I may show you a road to the heart of Twala's host."</content>
<content>There was a moment's pause, then suddenly a murmur arose from the
serried phalanxes before us, a sound like the distant whisper of the
sea, caused by the gentle tapping of the handles of six thousand spears
against their holders' shields.</content>
<content>Slowly it swelled, till its growing
volume deepened and widened into a roar of rolling noise, that echoed
like thunder against the mountains, and filled the air with heavy waves
of sound.</content>
<content>Then it decreased, and by faint degrees died away into
nothing, and suddenly out crashed the royal salute.</content>
<content>Ignosi, I thought to myself, might well be a proud man that day, for no
Roman emperor ever had such a salutation from gladiators "about to die."</content>
<content>Ignosi acknowledged this magnificent act of homage by lifting his
battle-axe, and then the Greys filed off in a triple-line formation,
each line containing about one thousand fighting men, exclusive of
officers.</content>
<content>When the last companies had advanced some five hundred yards,
Ignosi put himself at the head of the Buffaloes, which regiment was
drawn up in a similar three-fold formation, and gave the word to march,
and off we went, I, needless to say, uttering the most heartfelt
prayers that I might emerge from that entertainment with a whole skin.</content>
<content>Many a queer position have I found myself in, but never before in one
quite so unpleasant as the present, or one in which my chance of coming
off safe was smaller.</content>
<content>By the time that we reached the edge of the plateau the Greys were
already half-way down the slope ending in the tongue of grass land that
ran up into the bend of the mountain, something as the frog of a
horse's foot runs up into the shoe.</content>
<content>The excitement in Twala's camp on
the plain beyond was very great, and regiment after regiment was
starting forward at a long swinging trot in order to reach the root of
the tongue of land before the attacking force could emerge into the
plain of Loo.</content>
<content>This tongue, which was some four hundred yards in depth, even at its
root or widest part was not more than six hundred and fifty paces
across, while at its tip it scarcely measured ninety.</content>
<content>The Greys, who,
in passing down the side of the hill and on to the tip of the tongue,
had formed into a column, on reaching the spot where it broadened out
again, reassumed their triple-line formation, and halted dead.</content>
<content>Then we - that is, the Buffaloes - moved down the tip of the tongue and
took our stand in reserve, about one hundred yards behind the last line
of the Greys, and on slightly higher ground.</content>
<content>Meanwhile we had leisure
to observe Twala's entire force, which evidently had been reinforced
since the morning attack, and could not now, notwithstanding their
losses, number less than forty thousand, moving swiftly up towards us.</content>
<content>But as they drew near the root of the tongue they hesitated, having
discovered that only one regiment could advance into the gorge at a
time, and that there, some seventy yards from the mouth of it,
unassailable except in front, on account of the high walls of
boulder-strewn ground on each side, stood the famous regiment of Greys,
the pride and glory of the Kukuana army, ready to hold the way against
their power as the three Romans once held the bridge against thousands.</content>
<content>They hesitated, and finally stopped their advance; there was no
eagerness to cross spears with these three grim ranks of warriors who
stood so firm and ready.</content>
<content>Presently, however, a tall general, wearing
the customary head-dress of nodding ostrich plumes, appeared, attended
by a group of chiefs and orderlies, being, I thought, none other than
Twala himself.</content>
<content>He gave an order, and the first regiment, raising a
shout, charged up towards the Greys, who remained perfectly still and
silent till the attacking troops were within forty yards, and a volley
of tollas , or throwing-knives, came rattling among their ranks.</content>
<content>Then suddenly with a bound and a roar, they sprang forward with
uplifted spears, and the regiment met in deadly strife.</content>
<content>Next second the
roll of the meeting shields came to our ears like the sound of thunder,
and the plain seemed to be alive with flashes of light reflected from
the shimmering spears.</content>
<content>To and fro swung the surging mass of struggling,
stabbing humanity, but not for long.</content>
<content>Suddenly the attacking lines began
to grow thinner, and then with a slow, long heave the Greys passed over
them, just as a great wave heaves up its bulk and passes over a sunken
ridge.</content>
<content>It was done; that regiment was completely destroyed, but the
Greys had but two lines left now; a third of their number were dead.</content>
<content>Closing up shoulder to shoulder, once more they halted in silence and
awaited attack; and I was rejoiced to catch sight of Sir Henry's yellow
beard as he moved to and fro arranging the ranks.</content>
<content>So he was yet alive!</content>
<content>Meanwhile we moved on to the ground of the encounter, which was
cumbered by about four thousand prostrate human beings, dead, dying,
and wounded, and literally stained red with blood.</content>
<content>Ignosi issued an
order, which was rapidly passed down the ranks, to the effect that none
of the enemy's wounded were to be killed, and so far as we could see
this command was scrupulously carried out.</content>
<content>It would have been a
shocking sight, if we had found time to think of such things.</content>
<content>But now a second regiment, distinguished by white plumes, kilts, and
shields, was moving to the attack of the two thousand remaining Greys,
who stood waiting in the same ominous silence as before, till the foe
was within forty yards or so, when they hurled themselves with
irresistible force upon them.</content>
<content>Again there came the awful roll of the
meeting shields, and as we watched the tragedy repeated itself.</content>
<content>But this time the issue was left longer in doubt; indeed, it seemed for
awhile almost impossible that the Greys should again prevail.</content>
<content>The
attacking regiment, which was formed of young men, fought with the
utmost fury, and at first seemed by sheer weight to be driving the
veterans back.</content>
<content>The slaughter was truly awful, hundreds falling every
minute; and from among the shouts of the warriors and the groans of the
dying, set to the music of clashing spears, came a continuous hissing
undertone of " S'gee, s'gee ," the note of triumph of each victor as he
passed his assegai through and through the body of his fallen foe.</content>
<content>But perfect discipline and steady and unchanging valour can do wonders,
and one veteran soldier is worth two young ones, as soon became
apparent in the present case.</content>
<content>For just when we thought that it was all
over with the Greys, and were preparing to take their place so soon as
they made room by being destroyed, I heard Sir Henry's deep voice
ringing out through the din, and caught a glimpse of his circling
battle-axe as he waved it high above his plumes.</content>
<content>Then came a change;
the Greys ceased to give; they stood still as a rock, against which the
furious waves of spearmen broke again and again, only to recoil.</content>
<content>Presently they began to move once more - forward this time; as they had
no firearms there was no smoke, so we could see it all.</content>
