<content>Another minute
and the onslaught grew fainter.</content>
<content>"Ah, these are men , indeed; they will conquer again," called out
Ignosi, who was grinding his teeth with excitement at my side.</content>
<content>"See, it
is done!"</content>
<content>Suddenly, like puffs of smoke from the mouth of a cannon, the attacking
regiment broke away in flying groups, their white head-dresses
streaming behind them in the wind, and left their opponents victors,
indeed, but, alas!</content>
<content>no more a regiment.</content>
<content>Of the gallant triple line,
which forty minutes before had gone into action three thousand strong,
there remained at most some six hundred blood-spattered men; the rest
were under foot.</content>
<content>And yet they cheered and waved their spears in
triumph, and then, instead of falling back upon us as we expected, they
ran forward, for a hundred yards or so, after the flying groups of
foemen, took possession of a rising knoll of ground, and, resuming
their triple formation, formed a threefold ring around its base.</content>
<content>And
there, thanks be to Heaven, standing on the top of the mound for a
minute, I saw Sir Henry, apparently unharmed, and with him our old
friend Infadoos.</content>
<content>Then Twala's regiments rolled down upon the doomed
band, and once more the battle closed in.</content>
<content>As those who read this history will probably long ago have gathered, I
am, to be honest, a bit of a coward, and certainly in no way given to
fighting, though somehow it has often been my lot to get into
unpleasant positions, and to be obliged to shed man's blood.</content>
<content>But I have
always hated it, and kept my own blood as undiminished in quantity as
possible, sometimes by a judicious use of my heels.</content>
<content>At this moment,
however, for the first time in my life, I felt my bosom burn with
martial ardour.</content>
<content>Warlike fragments from the "Ingoldsby Legends,"
together with numbers of sanguinary verses in the Old Testament, sprang
up in my brain like mushrooms in the dark; my blood, which hitherto had
been half-frozen with horror, went beating through my veins, and there
came upon me a savage desire to kill and spare not.</content>
<content>I glanced round at
the serried ranks of warriors behind us, and somehow, all in an
instant, I began to wonder if my face looked like theirs.</content>
<content>There they
stood, the hands twitching, the lips apart, the fierce features
instinct with the hungry lust of battle, and in the eyes a look like
the glare of a bloodhound when after long pursuit he sights his quarry.</content>
<content>Only Ignosi's heart, to judge from his comparative self-possession,
seemed, to all appearances, to beat as calmly as ever beneath his
leopard-skin cloak, though even he still ground his teeth.</content>
<content>I could
bear it no longer.</content>
<content>"Are we to stand here till we put out roots, Umbopa - Ignosi, I
mean - while Twala swallows our brothers yonder?"</content>
<content>I asked.</content>
<content>"Nay, Macumazahn," was the answer; 'see, now is the ripe moment: let us
pluck it."</content>
<content>As he spoke a fresh regiment rushed past the ring upon the little
mound, and wheeling round, attacked it from the hither side.</content>
<content>Then, lifting his battle-axe, Ignosi gave the signal to advance, and,
screaming the wild Kukuana war-cry, the Buffaloes charged home with a
rush like the rush of the sea.</content>
<content>What followed immediately on this it is out of my power to tell.</content>
<content>All I
can remember is an irregular yet ordered advance, that seemed to shake
the ground; a sudden change of front and forming up on the part of the
regiment against which the charge was directed; then an awful shock, a
dull roar of voices, and a continuous flashing of spears, seen through
a red mist of blood.</content>
<content>When my mind cleared I found myself standing inside the remnant of the
Greys near the top of the mound, and just behind no less a person than
Sir Henry himself.</content>
<content>How I got there I had at the moment no idea, but Sir
Henry afterwards told me that I was borne up by the first furious
charge of the Buffaloes almost to his feet, and then left, as they in
turn were pressed back.</content>
<content>Thereon he dashed out of the circle and dragged
me into shelter.</content>
<content>As for the fight that followed, who can describe it?</content>
<content>Again and again
the multitudes surged against our momentarily lessening circle, and
again and again we beat them back.</content>
<content>"The stubborn spearmen still made good
 The dark impenetrable wood,
 Each stepping where his comrade stood
 The instant that he fell,"

as someone or other beautifully says.</content>
<content>It was a splendid thing to see those brave battalions come on time
after time over the barriers of their dead, sometimes lifting corpses
before them to receive our spear-thrusts, only to leave their own
corpses to swell the rising piles.</content>
<content>It was a gallant sight to see that
old warrior, Infadoos, as cool as though he were on parade, shouting
out orders, taunts, and even jests, to keep up the spirit of his few
remaining men, and then, as each charge rolled on, stepping forward to
wherever the fighting was thickest, to bear his share in its repulse.</content>
<content>And yet more gallant was the vision of Sir Henry, whose ostrich plumes
had been shorn off by a spear thrust, so that his long yellow hair
streamed out in the breeze behind him.</content>
<content>There he stood, the great Dane,
for he was nothing else, his hands, his axe, and his armour all red
with blood, and none could live before his stroke.</content>
<content>Time after time I
saw it sweeping down, as some great warrior ventured to give him
battle, and as he struck he shouted " O-hoy!</content>
<content>O-hoy! "</content>
<content>like his
Berserkir forefathers, and the blow went crashing through shield and
spear, through head-dress, hair, and skull, till at last none would of
their own will come near the great white " umtagati ," the wizard, who
killed and failed not.</content>
<content>But suddenly there rose a cry of " Twala, y' Twala ," and out of the
press sprang forward none other than the gigantic one-eyed king
himself, also armed with battle-axe and shield, and clad in chain
armour.</content>
<content>"Where art thou, Incubu, thou white man, who slewest Scragga my
son - see if thou canst slay me!"</content>
<content>he shouted, and at the same time
hurled a tolla straight at Sir Henry, who fortunately saw it coming,
and caught it on his shield, which it transfixed, remaining wedged in
the iron plate behind the hide.</content>
<content>Then, with a cry, Twala sprang forward straight at him, and with his
battle-axe struck him such a blow upon the shield that the mere force
and shock of it brought Sir Henry, strong man as he is, down upon his
knees.</content>
<content>But at this time the matter went no further, for that instant there
rose from the regiments pressing round us something like a shout of
dismay, and on looking up I saw the cause.</content>
<content>To the right and to the left the plain was alive with the plumes of
charging warriors.</content>
<content>The outflanking squadrons had come to our relief.</content>
<content>The time could not have been better chosen.</content>
<content>All Twala's army, as Ignosi
predicted would be the case, had fixed their attention on the bloody
struggle which was raging round the remnant of the Greys and that of
the Buffaloes, who were now carrying on a battle of their own at a
little distance, which two regiments had formed the chest of our army.</content>
<content>It was not until our horns were about to close upon them that they had
dreamed of their approach, for they believed these forces to be hidden
in reserve upon the crest of the moon-shaped hill.</content>
<content>And now, before they
could even assume a proper formation for defence, the outflanking
 Impis had leapt, like greyhounds, on their flanks.</content>
<content>In five minutes the fate of the battle was decided.</content>
<content>Taken on both
flanks, and dismayed at the awful slaughter inflicted upon them by the
Greys and Buffaloes, Twala's regiments broke into flight, and soon the
whole plain between us and Loo was scattered with groups of running
soldiers making good their retreat.</content>
<content>As for the hosts that had so
recently surrounded us and the Buffaloes, they melted away as though by
magic, and presently we were left standing there like a rock from which
the sea has retreated.</content>
<content>But what a sight it was!</content>
<content>Around us the dead and
dying lay in heaped-up masses, and of the gallant Greys there remained
but ninety-five men upon their feet.</content>
<content>More than three thousand four
hundred had fallen in this one regiment, most of them never to rise
again.</content>
<content>"Men," said Infadoos calmly, as between the intervals of binding a
wound on his arm he surveyed what remained to him of his corps, "ye
have kept up the reputation of your regiment, and this day's fighting
will be well spoken of by your children's children."</content>
<content>Then he turned
round and shook Sir Henry Curtis by the hand.</content>
<content>"Thou art a great
captain, Incubu," he said simply; "I have lived a long life among
warriors, and have known many a brave one, yet have I never seen a man
like unto thee."</content>
<content>At this moment the Buffaloes began to march past our position on the
road to Loo, and as they went a message was brought to us from Ignosi
requesting Infadoos, Sir Henry, and myself to join them.</content>
<content>Accordingly,
orders having been issued to the remaining ninety men of the Greys to
employ themselves in collecting the wounded, we joined Ignosi, who
informed us that he was pressing on to Loo to complete the victory by
capturing Twala, if that should be possible.</content>
<content>Before we had gone far,
suddenly we discovered the figure of Good sitting on an ant-heap about
one hundred paces from us.</content>
<content>Close beside him was the body of a Kukuana.</content>
<content>"He must be wounded," said Sir Henry anxiously.</content>
<content>As he made the remark,
an untoward thing happened.</content>
<content>The dead body of the Kukuana soldier, or
rather what had appeared to be his dead body, suddenly sprang up,
knocked Good head over heels off the ant-heap, and began to spear him.</content>
<content>We rushed forward in terror, and as we drew near we saw the brawny
warrior making dig after dig at the prostrate Good, who at each prod
jerked all his limbs into the air.</content>
<content>Seeing us coming, the Kukuana gave
one final and most vicious dig, and with a shout of "Take that,
wizard!"</content>
<content>bolted away.</content>
<content>Good did not move, and we concluded that our poor
comrade was done for.</content>
<content>Sadly we came towards him, and were astonished to
find him pale and faint indeed, but with a serene smile upon his face,
and his eyeglass still fixed in his eye.</content>
<content>"Capital armour this," he murmured, on catching sight of our faces
bending over him.</content>
<content>"How sold that beggar must have been," and then he
fainted.</content>
<content>On examination we discovered that he had been seriously
wounded in the leg by a tolla in the course of the pursuit, but that
the chain armour had prevented his last assailant's spear from doing
anything more than bruise him badly.</content>
<content>It was a merciful escape.</content>
<content>As
nothing could be done for him at the moment, he was placed on one of
the wicker shields used for the wounded, and carried along with us.</content>
<content>On arriving before the nearest gate of Loo we found one of our
regiments watching it in obedience to orders received from Ignosi.</content>
<content>The
other regiments were in the same way guarding the different exits to
the town.</content>
<content>The officer in command of this regiment saluted Ignosi as
king, and informed him that Twala's army had taken refuge in the town,
whither Twala himself had also escaped, but he thought that they were
thoroughly demoralised, and would surrender.</content>
<content>Thereupon Ignosi, after
taking counsel with us, sent forward heralds to each gate ordering the
defenders to open, and promising on his royal word life and forgiveness
to every soldier who laid down his arms, but saying that if they did
not do so before nightfall he would certainly burn the town and all
within its gates.</content>
<content>This message was not without its effect.</content>
<content>Half an hour
later, amid the shouts and cheers of the Buffaloes, the bridge was
dropped across the fosse, and the gates upon the further side were
flung open.</content>
<content>Taking due precautions against treachery, we marched on into the town.</content>
<content>All along the roadways stood thousands of dejected warriors, their
heads drooping, and their shields and spears at their feet, who, headed
by their officers, saluted Ignosi as king as he passed.</content>
<content>On we marched,
straight to Twala's kraal.</content>
<content>When we reached the great space, where a day
or two previously we had seen the review and the witch hunt, we found
it deserted.</content>
<content>No, not quite deserted, for there, on the further side, in
front of his hut, sat Twala himself, with but one attendant - Gagool.</content>
<content>It was a melancholy sight to see him seated, his battle-axe and shield
by his side, his chin upon his mailed breast, with but one old crone
for companion, and notwithstanding his crimes and misdeeds, a pang of
compassion shot through me as I looked upon Twala thus "fallen from his
high estate."</content>
<content>Not a soldier of all his armies, not a courtier out of
the hundreds who had cringed round him, not even a solitary wife,
remained to share his fate or halve the bitterness of his fall.</content>
<content>Poor
savage!</content>
<content>he was learning the lesson which Fate teaches to most of us who
live long enough, that the eyes of mankind are blind to the
discredited, and that he who is defenceless and fallen finds few
friends and little mercy.</content>
<content>Nor, indeed, in this case did he deserve any.</content>
<content>Filing through the kraal gate, we marched across the open space to
where the ex-king sat.</content>
<content>When within about fifty yards of him the
regiment was halted, and accompanied only by a small guard we advanced
towards him, Gagool reviling us bitterly as we came.</content>
<content>As we drew near,
Twala, for the first time, lifted his plumed head, and fixed his one
eye, which seemed to flash with suppressed fury almost as brightly as
the great diamond bound round his forehead, upon his successful
rival - Ignosi.</content>
<content>"Hail, O king!"</content>
<content>he said, with bitter mockery; "thou who hast eaten of
my bread, and now by the aid of the white man's magic hast seduced my
regiments and defeated mine army, hail!</content>
<content>What fate hast thou in store
for me, O king?"</content>
<content>"The fate thou gavest to my father, whose throne thou hast sat on these
many years!"</content>
<content>was the stern answer.</content>
<content>"It is good.</content>
<content>I will show thee how to die, that thou mayest remember it
against thine own time.</content>
<content>See, the sun sinks in blood," and he pointed
with his battle-axe towards the setting orb; "it is well that my sun
should go down in its company.</content>
<content>And now, O king!</content>
<content>I am ready to die, but
I crave the boon of the Kukuana royal House 1  to die fighting.</content>
<content>Thou
canst not refuse it, or even those cowards who fled to-day will hold thee
shamed."</content>
<content>"It is granted.</content>
<content>Choose - with whom wilt thou fight?</content>
<content>Myself I cannot
fight with thee, for the king fights not except in war."</content>
<content>Twala's sombre eye ran up and down our ranks, and I felt, as for a
moment it rested on myself, that the position had developed a new
horror.</content>
<content>What if he chose to begin by fighting me ?</content>
<content>What chance should
I have against a desperate savage six feet five high, and broad in
proportion?</content>
<content>I might as well commit suicide at once.</content>
<content>Hastily I made up
my mind to decline the combat, even if I were hooted out of Kukuanaland
as a consequence.</content>
<content>It is, I think, better to be hooted than to be
quartered with a battle-axe.</content>
<content>Presently Twala spoke.</content>
<content>"Incubu, what sayest thou, shall we end what we began to-day, or shall
I call thee coward, white - even to the liver?"</content>
<content>"Nay," interposed Ignosi hastily; "thou shalt not fight with Incubu."</content>
<content>"Not if he is afraid," said Twala.</content>
<content>Unfortunately Sir Henry understood this remark, and the blood flamed up
into his cheeks.</content>
<content>"I will fight him," he said; "he shall see if I am afraid."</content>
<content>"For Heaven's sake," I entreated, "don't  risk your life against that of
a desperate man.</content>
<content>Anybody who saw you to-day will know that you are
brave enough."</content>
<content>"I will fight him," was the sullen answer.</content>
<content>"No living man shall call me
a coward.</content>
<content>I am ready now!"</content>
<content>and he stepped forward and lifted his axe.</content>
<content>I wrung my hands over this absurd piece of Quixotism; but if he was
determined on this deed, of course I could not stop him.</content>
<content>"Fight not, my white brother," said Ignosi, laying his hand
affectionately on Sir Henry's arm; "thou hast fought enough, and if
aught befell thee at his hands it would cut my heart in twain."</content>
<content>"I will fight, Ignosi," was Sir Henry's answer.</content>
<content>"It is well, Incubu; thou art a brave man.</content>
<content>It will be a good fray.</content>
<content>Behold, Twala, the Elephant is ready for thee."</content>
<content>The ex-king laughed savagely, and stepping forward faced Curtis.</content>
<content>For a
moment they stood thus, and the light of the sinking sun caught their
stalwart frames and clothed them both in fire.</content>
<content>They were a well-matched
pair.</content>
<content>Then they began to circle round each other, their battle-axes raised.</content>
<content>Suddenly Sir Henry sprang forward and struck a fearful blow at Twala,
who stepped to one side.</content>
<content>So heavy was the stroke that the striker half
overbalanced himself, a circumstance of which his antagonist took a
prompt advantage.</content>
<content>Circling his massive battle-axe round his head, he
brought it down with tremendous force.</content>
<content>My heart jumped into my mouth; I
thought that the affair was already finished.</content>
<content>But no; with a quick
upward movement of the left arm Sir Henry interposed his shield between
himself and the axe, with the result that its outer edge was shorn
away, the axe falling on his left shoulder, but not heavily enough to
do any serious damage.</content>
<content>In another moment Sir Henry got in a second
blow, which was also received by Twala upon his shield.</content>
<content>Then followed blow upon blow, that were, in turn, either received upon
the shields or avoided.</content>
<content>The excitement grew intense; the regiment which
was watching the encounter forgot its discipline, and, drawing near,
shouted and groaned at every stroke.</content>
<content>Just at this time, too, Good, who
had been laid upon the ground by me, recovered from his faint, and,
sitting up, perceived what was going on.</content>
<content>In an instant he was up, and
catching hold of my arm, hopped about from place to place on one leg,
dragging me after him, and yelling encouragements to Sir Henry - 

"Go it, old fellow!"</content>
<content>he hallooed.</content>
<content>"That was a good one!</content>
<content>Give it him
amidships," and so on.</content>
<content>Presently Sir Henry, having caught a fresh stroke upon his shield, hit
out with all his force.</content>
<content>The blow cut through Twala's shield and through
the tough chain armour behind it, gashing him in the shoulder.</content>
<content>With a
yell of pain and fury Twala returned the blow with interest, and, such
was his strength, shore right through the rhinoceros' horn handle of
his antagonists battle-axe, strengthened as it was with bands of steel,
wounding Curtis in the face.</content>
<content>A cry of dismay rose from the Buffaloes as our hero's broad axe-head
fell to the ground; and Twala, again raising his weapon, flew at him
with a shout.</content>
<content>I shut my eyes.</content>
<content>When I opened them again it was to see
Sir Henry's shield lying on the ground, and Sir Henry himself with his
great arms twined round Twala's middle.</content>
<content>To and fro they swung, hugging
each other like bears, straining with all their mighty muscles for dear
life, and dearer honour.</content>
<content>With a supreme effort Twala swung the
Englishman clean off his feet, and down they came together, rolling
over and over on the lime paving, Twala striking out at Curtis' head
with the battle-axe, and Sir Henry trying to drive the tolla he had
drawn from his belt through Twala's armour.</content>
<content>It was a mighty struggle, and an awful thing to see.</content>
<content>"Get his axe!"</content>
<content>yelled Good; and perhaps our champion heard him.</content>
<content>At any rate, dropping the tolla , he snatched at the axe, which was
fastened to Twala's wrist by a strip of buffalo hide, and still rolling
over and over, they fought for it like wild cats, drawing their breath
in heavy gasps.</content>
<content>Suddenly the hide string burst, and then, with a great
effort, Sir Henry freed himself, the weapon remaining in his hand.</content>
<content>Another second and he was upon his feet, the red blood streaming from
the wound in his face, and so was Twala.</content>
<content>Drawing the heavy tolla from
his belt, he reeled straight at Curtis and struck him in the breast.</content>
<content>The stab came home true and strong, but whoever it was who made that
chain armour, he understood his art, for it withstood the steel.</content>
<content>Again
Twala struck out with a savage yell, and again the sharp knife
rebounded, and Sir Henry went staggering back.</content>
<content>Once more Twala came on,
and as he came our great Englishman gathered himself together, and
swinging the big axe round his head with both hands, hit at him with
all his force.</content>
<content>There was a shriek of excitement from a thousand throats, and, behold!</content>
<content>Twala's head seemed to spring from his shoulders: then it fell and came
rolling and bounding along the ground towards Ignosi, stopping just at
his feet.</content>
<content>For a second the corpse stood upright; then with a dull crash
it came to the earth, and the gold torque from its neck rolled away
across the pavement.</content>
<content>As it did so Sir Henry, overpowered by faintness
and loss of blood, fell heavily across the body of the dead king.</content>
<content>In a second he was lifted up, and eager hands were pouring water on his
face.</content>
<content>Another minute, and the grey eyes opened wide.</content>
<content>He was not dead.</content>
<content>Then I, just as the sun sank, stepping to where Twala's head lay in the
dust, unloosed the diamond from the dead brows, and handed it to Ignosi.</content>
<content>"Take it," I said, "lawful king of the Kukuanas - king by birth and
victory."</content>
<content>Ignosi bound the diadem upon his brows.</content>
<content>Then advancing, he placed his
foot upon the broad chest of his headless foe and broke out into a
chant, or rather a pÃ¦an of triumph, so beautiful, and yet so utterly
savage, that I despair of being able to give an adequate version of his
words.</content>
<content>Once I heard a scholar with a fine voice read aloud from the
Greek poet Homer, and I remember that the sound of the rolling lines
seemed to make my blood stand still.</content>
<content>Ignosi's chant, uttered as it was
in a language as beautiful and sonorous as the old Greek, produced
exactly the same effect on me, although I was exhausted with toil and
many emotions.</content>
<content>"Now," he began, "now our rebellion is swallowed up in victory, and our
evil-doing is justified by strength.</content>
<content>"In the morning the oppressors arose and stretched themselves; they
bound on their harness and made them ready to war.</content>
<content>"They rose up and tossed their spears: the soldiers called to the
captains, 'Come, lead us' - and the captains cried to the king, 'Direct
thou the battle.'</content>
<content>"They laughed in their pride, twenty thousand men, and yet a twenty
thousand.</content>
<content>"Their plumes covered the valleys as the plumes of a bird cover her
nest; they shook their shields and shouted, yea, they shook their
shields in the sunlight; they lusted for battle and were glad.</content>
<content>"They came up against me; their strong ones ran swiftly to slay me;
they cried, 'Ha!</content>
<content>ha!</content>
<content>he is as one already dead.'</content>
<content>"Then breathed I on them, and my breath was as the breath of a wind,
and lo!</content>
<content>they were not.</content>
<content>"My lightnings pierced them; I licked up their strength with the
lightning of my spears; I shook them to the ground with the thunder of
my shoutings.</content>
<content>"They broke - they scattered - they were gone as the mists of the morning.</content>
<content>"They are food for the kites and the foxes, and the place of battle is
fat with their blood.</content>
<content>"Where are the mighty ones who rose up in the morning?</content>
<content>"Where are the proud ones who tossed their spears and cried, 'He is as
a man already dead'?</content>
<content>"They bow their heads, but not in sleep; they are stretched out, but
not in sleep.</content>
<content>"They are forgotten; they have gone into the blackness; they dwell in
the dead moons; yea, others shall lead away their wives, and their
children shall remember them no more.</content>
<content>"And I - !</content>
<content>the king - like an eagle I have found my eyrie.</content>
<content>"Behold!</content>
<content>far have I flown in the night season, yet have I returned to
my young at the daybreak.</content>
<content>"Shelter ye under the shadow of my wings, O people, and I will comfort
you, and ye shall not be dismayed.</content>
<content>"Now is the good time, the time of spoil.</content>
<content>"Mine are the cattle on the mountains, mine are the virgins in the
kraals.</content>
<content>"The winter is overpast with storms, the summer is come with flowers.</content>
<content>"Now Evil shall cover up her face, now Mercy and Gladness shall dwell
in the land.</content>
<content>"Rejoice, rejoice, my people!</content>
<content>"Let all the stars rejoice in that this tyranny is trodden down, in
that I am the king."</content>
<content>Ignosi ceased his song, and out of the gathering gloom came back the
deep reply - 

" Thou art the king! "</content>
<content>Thus was my prophecy to the herald fulfilled, and within the
forty-eight hours Twala's headless corpse was stiffening at Twala's
gate.</content>
<content>1  It is a law amongst the Kukuanas that no man of the direct royal
blood can be put to death, unless by his own consent, which is,
however, never refused.</content>
<content>He is allowed to choose a succession of
antagonists, to be approved by the king, with whom he fights, till one
of them kills him.</content>
<content>- A.Q.</content>
<content>CHAPTER XV

GOOD FALLS SICK

After the fight was ended, Sir Henry and Good were carried into Twala's
hut, where I joined them.</content>
<content>They were both utterly exhausted by exertion
and loss of blood, and, indeed, my own condition was little better.</content>
<content>I
am very wiry, and can stand more fatigue than most men, probably on
account of my light weight and long training; but that night I was
quite done up, and, as is always the case with me when exhausted, that
old wound which the lion gave me began to pain.</content>
<content>Also my head was aching
violently from the blow I had received in the morning, when I was
knocked senseless.</content>
<content>Altogether, a more miserable trio than we were that
evening it would have been difficult to discover; and our only comfort
lay in the reflection that we were exceedingly fortunate to be there to
feel miserable, instead of being stretched dead upon the plain, as so
many thousands of brave men were that night, who had risen well and
strong in the morning.</content>
<content>Somehow, with the assistance of the beautiful Foulata, who, since we
had been the means of saving her life, had constituted herself our
handmaiden, and especially Good's, we managed to get off the chain
shirts, which had certainly saved the lives of two of us that day.</content>
<content>As I
expected, we found that the flesh underneath was terribly contused, for
though the steel links had kept the weapons from entering, they had not
prevented them from bruising.</content>
<content>Both Sir Henry and Good were a mass of
contusions, and I was by no means free.</content>
<content>As a remedy Foulata brought us
some pounded green leaves, with an aromatic odour, which, when applied
as a plaster, gave us considerable relief.</content>
<content>But though the bruises were painful, they did not give us such anxiety
as Sir Henry's and Good's wounds.</content>
<content>Good had a hole right through the
fleshy part of his "beautiful white leg," from which he had lost a
great deal of blood; and Sir Henry, with other hurts, had a deep cut
over the jaw, inflicted by Twala's battle-axe.</content>
<content>Luckily Good is a very
decent surgeon, and so soon as his small box of medicines was
forthcoming, having thoroughly cleansed the wounds, he managed to
stitch up first Sir Henry's and then his own pretty satisfactorily,
considering the imperfect light given by the primitive Kukuana lamp in
the hut.</content>
<content>Afterwards he plentifully smeared the injured places with some
antiseptic ointment, of which there was a pot in the little box, and we
covered them with the remains of a pocket-handkerchief which we
possessed.</content>
<content>Meanwhile Foulata had prepared us some strong broth, for we were too
weary to eat.</content>
<content>This we swallowed, and then threw ourselves down on the
piles of magnificent karrosses, or fur rugs, which were scattered about
the dead king's great hut.</content>
<content>By a very strange instance of the irony of
fate, it was on Twala's own couch, and wrapped in Twala's own
particular karross, that Sir Henry, the man who had slain him, slept
that night.</content>
<content>I say slept; but after that day's work, sleep was indeed difficult.</content>
<content>To
begin with, in very truth the air was full

 "Of farewells to the dying
 And mournings for the dead."</content>
<content>From every direction came the sound of the wailing of women whose
husbands, sons, and brothers had perished in the battle.</content>
<content>No wonder that
they wailed, for over twelve thousand men, or nearly a fifth of the
Kukuana army, had been destroyed in that awful struggle.</content>
<content>It was
heart-rending to lie and listen to their cries for those who never
would return; and it made me understand the full horror of the work
done that day to further man's ambition.</content>
<content>Towards midnight, however, the
ceaseless crying of the women grew less frequent, till at length the
silence was only broken at intervals of a few minutes by a long
piercing howl that came from a hut in our immediate rear, which, as I
afterwards discovered, proceeded from Gagool "keening" over the dead
king Twala.</content>
<content>After that I got a little fitful sleep, only to wake from time to time
with a start, thinking that I was once more an actor in the terrible
events of the last twenty-four hours.</content>
<content>Now I seemed to see that warrior
whom my hand had sent to his last account charging at me on the
mountain-top; now I was once more in that glorious ring of Greys, which
made its immortal stand against all Twala's regiments upon the little
mound; and now again I saw Twala's plumed and gory head roll past my
feet with gnashing teeth and glaring eye.</content>
<content>At last, somehow or other, the night passed away; but when dawn broke I
found that my companions had slept no better than myself.</content>
<content>Good, indeed,
was in a high fever, and very soon afterwards began to grow
light-headed, and also, to my alarm, to spit blood, the result, no
doubt, of some internal injury, inflicted during the desperate efforts
made by the Kukuana warrior on the previous day to force his big spear
through the chain armour.</content>
<content>Sir Henry, however, seemed pretty fresh,
notwithstanding his wound on the face, which made eating difficult and
laughter an impossibility, though he was so sore and stiff that he
could scarcely stir.</content>
<content>About eight o'clock we had a visit from Infadoos, who appeared but
little the worse - tough old warrior that he was - for his exertions in
the battle, although he informed us that he had been up all night.</content>
<content>He
was delighted to see us, but much grieved at Good's condition, and
shook our hands cordially.</content>
<content>I noticed, however, that he addressed Sir
Henry with a kind of reverence, as though he were something more than
man; and, indeed, as we afterwards found out, the great Englishman was
looked on throughout Kukuanaland as a supernatural being.</content>
<content>No man, the
soldiers said, could have fought as he fought or, at the end of a day
of such toil and bloodshed, could have slain Twala, who, in addition to
being the king, was supposed to be the strongest warrior in the
country, in single combat, shearing through his bull-neck at a stroke.</content>
<content>Indeed, that stroke became proverbial in Kukuanaland, and any
extraordinary blow or feat of strength was henceforth known as
"Incubu's blow."</content>
<content>Infadoos told us also that all Twala's regiments had submitted to
Ignosi, and that like submissions were beginning to arrive from chiefs
in the outlying country.</content>
<content>Twala's death at the hands of Sir Henry had
put an end to all further chance of disturbance; for Scragga had been
his only legitimate son, so there was no rival claimant to the throne
left alive.</content>
<content>I remarked that Ignosi had swum to power through blood.</content>
<content>The old chief
shrugged his shoulders.</content>
<content>"Yes," he answered; "but the Kukuana people can
only be kept cool by letting their blood flow sometimes.</content>
<content>Many are
killed, indeed, but the women are left, and others must soon grow up to
take the places of the fallen.</content>
<content>After this the land would be quiet for a
while."</content>
<content>Afterwards, in the course of the morning, we had a short visit from
Ignosi, on whose brows the royal diadem was now bound.</content>
<content>As I
contemplated him advancing with kingly dignity, an obsequious guard
following his steps, I could not help recalling to my mind the tall
Zulu who had presented himself to us at Durban some few months back,
asking to be taken into our service, and reflecting on the strange
revolutions of the wheel of fortune.</content>
<content>"Hail, O king!"</content>
<content>I said, rising.</content>
<content>"Yes, Macumazahn.</content>
<content>King at last, by the might of your three right
hands," was the ready answer.</content>
<content>All was, he said, going well; and he hoped to arrange a great feast in
two weeks' time in order to show himself to the people.</content>
<content>I asked him what he had settled to do with Gagool.</content>
<content>"She is the evil genius of the land," he answered, "and I shall kill
her, and all the witch doctors with her!</content>
<content>She has lived so long that
none can remember when she was not very old, and she it is who has
always trained the witch-hunters, and made the land wicked in the sight
of the heavens above."</content>
<content>"Yet she knows much," I replied; "it is easier to destroy knowledge,
Ignosi, than to gather it."</content>
<content>"That is so," he said thoughtfully.</content>
<content>"She, and she only, knows the
secret of the 'Three Witches,' yonder, whither the great road runs,
where the kings are buried, and the Silent Ones sit."</content>
<content>"Yes, and the diamonds are.</content>
<content>Forget not thy promise, Ignosi; thou must
lead us to the mines, even if thou hast to spare Gagool alive to show
the way."</content>
<content>"I will not forget, Macumazahn, and I will think on what thou sayest."</content>
<content>After Ignosi's visit I went to see Good, and found him quite delirious.</content>
<content>The fever set up by his wound seemed to have taken a firm hold of his
system, and to be complicated with an internal injury.</content>
<content>For four or five
days his condition was most critical; indeed, I believe firmly that had
it not been for Foulata's indefatigable nursing he must have died.</content>
<content>Women are women, all the world over, whatever their colour.</content>
<content>Yet somehow
it seemed curious to watch this dusky beauty bending night and day over
the fevered man's couch, and performing all the merciful errands of a
sick-room swiftly, gently, and with as fine an instinct as that of a
trained hospital nurse.</content>
<content>For the first night or two I tried to help her,
and so did Sir Henry as soon as his stiffness allowed him to move, but
Foulata bore our interference with impatience, and finally insisted
upon our leaving him to her, saying that our movements made him
restless, which I think was true.</content>
<content>Day and night she watched him and
tended him, giving him his only medicine, a native cooling drink made
of milk, in which was infused juice from the bulb of a species of
tulip, and keeping the flies from settling on him.</content>
<content>I can see the whole
picture now as it appeared night after night by the light of our
primitive lamp; Good tossing to and fro, his features emaciated, his
eyes shining large and luminous, and jabbering nonsense by the yard;
and seated on the ground by his side, her back resting against the wall
of the hut, the soft-eyed, shapely Kukuana beauty, her face, weary as
it was with her long vigil, animated by a look of infinite
compassion - or was it something more than compassion?</content>
<content>For two days we thought that he must die, and crept about with heavy
hearts.</content>
<content>Only Foulata would not believe it.</content>
<content>"He will live," she said.</content>
<content>For three hundred yards or more around Twala's chief hut, where the
sufferer lay, there was silence; for by the king's order all who lived
in the habitations behind it, except Sir Henry and myself, had been
removed, lest any noise should come to the sick man's ears.</content>
<content>One night,
it was the fifth of Good's illness, as was my habit, I went across to
see how he was doing before turning in for a few hours.</content>
<content>I entered the hut carefully.</content>
<content>The lamp placed upon the floor showed the
figure of Good tossing no more, but lying quite still.</content>
<content>So it had come at last!</content>
<content>In the bitterness of my heart I gave something
like a sob.</content>
<content>"Hush - h - h!"</content>
<content>came from the patch of dark shadow behind Good's head.</content>
<content>Then, creeping closer, I saw that he was not dead, but sleeping
soundly, with Foulata's taper fingers clasped tightly in his poor white
hand.</content>
<content>The crisis had passed, and he would live.</content>
<content>He slept like that for
eighteen hours; and I scarcely like to say it, for fear I should not be
believed, but during the entire period did this devoted girl sit by
him, fearing that if she moved and drew away her hand it would wake
him.</content>
<content>What she must have suffered from cramp and weariness, to say
nothing of want of food, nobody will ever know; but it is the fact
that, when at last he woke, she had to be carried away - her limbs were
so stiff that she could not move them.</content>
<content>After the turn had once been taken, Good's recovery was rapid and
complete.</content>
<content>It was not till he was nearly well that Sir Henry told him of
all he owed to Foulata; and when he came to the story of how she sat by
his side for eighteen hours, fearing lest by moving she should wake
him, the honest sailor's eyes filled with tears.</content>
<content>He turned and went
straight to the hut where Foulata was preparing the mid-day meal, for
we were back in our old quarters now, taking me with him to interpret
in case he could not make his meaning clear to her, though I am bound
to say that she understood him marvellously as a rule, considering how
extremely limited was his foreign vocabulary.</content>
<content>"Tell her," said Good, "that I owe her my life, and that I will never
forget her kindness to my dying day."</content>
<content>I interpreted, and under her dark skin she actually seemed to blush.</content>
<content>Turning to him with one of those swift and graceful motions that in her
always reminded me of the flight of a wild bird, Foulata answered
softly, glancing at him with her large brown eyes - 

"Nay, my lord; my lord forgets!</content>
<content>Did he not save my life, and am I not
my lord's handmaiden?"</content>
<content>It will be observed that the young lady appeared entirely to have
forgotten the share which Sir Henry and myself had taken in her
preservation from Twala's clutches.</content>
<content>But that is the way of women!</content>
<content>I
remember my dear wife was just the same.</content>
<content>Well, I retired from that
little interview sad at heart.</content>
<content>I did not like Miss Foulata's soft
glances, for I knew the fatal amorous propensities of sailors in
general, and of Good in particular.</content>
<content>There are two things in the world, as I have found out, which cannot be
prevented: you cannot keep a Zulu from fighting, or a sailor from
falling in love upon the slightest provocation!</content>
<content>It was a few days after this last occurrence that Ignosi held his great
"indaba," or council, and was formally recognised as king by the
"indunas," or head men, of Kukuanaland.</content>
<content>The spectacle was a most
imposing one, including as it did a grand review of troops.</content>
<content>On this day
the remaining fragments of the Greys were formally paraded, and in the
face of the army thanked for their splendid conduct in the battle.</content>
<content>To
each man the king made a large present of cattle, promoting them one
and all to the rank of officers in the new corps of Greys which was in
process of formation.</content>
<content>An order was also promulgated throughout the
length and breadth of Kukuanaland that, whilst we honoured the country
by our presence, we three were to be greeted with the royal salute, and
to be treated with the same ceremony and respect that was by custom
accorded to the king.</content>
<content>Also the power of life and death was publicly
conferred upon us.</content>
<content>Ignosi, too, in the presence of his people,
reaffirmed the promises which he had made, to the effect that no man's
blood should be shed without trial, and that witch-hunting should cease
in the land.</content>
<content>When the ceremony was over we waited upon Ignosi, and informed him that
we were now anxious to investigate the mystery of the mines to which
Solomon's Road ran, asking him if he had discovered anything about them.</content>
<content>"My friends," he answered, "I have discovered this.</content>
<content>It is there that
the three great figures sit, who here are called the 'Silent Ones,' and
to whom Twala would have offered the girl Foulata as a sacrifice.</content>
<content>It is
there, too, in a great cave deep in the mountain, that the kings of the
land are buried; there ye shall find Twala's body, sitting with those
who went before him.</content>
<content>There, also, is a deep pit, which, at some time,
long-dead men dug out, mayhap for the stones ye speak of, such as I
have heard men in Natal tell of at Kimberley.</content>
<content>There, too, in the Place
of Death is a secret chamber, known to none but the king and Gagool.</content>
<content>But Twala, who knew it, is dead, and I know it not, nor know I what is
in it.</content>
<content>Yet there is a legend in the land that once, many generations
gone, a white man crossed the mountains, and was led by a woman to the
secret chamber and shown the wealth hidden in it.</content>
<content>But before he could
take it she betrayed him, and he was driven by the king of that day

<content>"The story is surely true, Ignosi, for on the mountains we found the

<content>"Yes, we found him.</content>
<content>And now I have promised you that if ye can come to

<content>CHAPTER XVI

THE PLACE OF DEATH

It was already dark on the third day after the scene described in the
previous chapter when we camped in some huts at the foot of the "Three
Witches," as the triangle of mountains is called to which Solomon's
Great Road runs.</content>
<content>Our party consisted of our three selves and Foulata,
who waited on us - especially on Good - Infadoos, Gagool, who was borne
along in a litter, inside which she could be heard muttering and
cursing all day long, and a party of guards and attendants.</content>
<content>The
mountains, or rather the three peaks of the mountain, for the mass was
evidently the result of a solitary upheaval, were, as I have said, in
the form of a triangle, of which the base was towards us, one peak
being on our right, one on our left, and one straight in front of us.</content>
<content>Never shall I forget the sight afforded by those three towering peaks
in the early sunlight of the following morning.</content>
<content>High, high above us, up
into the blue air, soared their twisted snow-wreaths.</content>
<content>Beneath the
snow-line the peaks were purple with heaths, and so were the wild moors
that ran up the slopes towards them.</content>
<content>Straight before us the white
ribbon of Solomon's Great Road stretched away uphill to the foot of the
centre peak, about five miles from us, and there stopped.</content>
<content>It was its
terminus.</content>
<content>I had better leave the feelings of intense excitement with which we set
out on our march that morning to the imagination of those who read this
history.</content>
<content>At last we were drawing near to the wonderful mines that had
been the cause of the miserable death of the old Portuguese Dom three
centuries ago, of my poor friend, his ill-starred descendant, and also,
as we feared, of George Curtis, Sir Henry's brother.</content>
<content>Were we destined,
after all that we had gone through, to fare any better?</content>
<content>Evil befell
them, as that old fiend Gagool said; would it also befall us?</content>
<content>Somehow,
as we were marching up that last stretch of beautiful road, I could not
help feeling a little superstitious about the matter, and so I think
did Good and Sir Henry.</content>
<content>For an hour and a half or more we tramped on up the heather-fringed
way, going so fast in our excitement that the bearers of Gagool's
hammock could scarcely keep pace with us, and its occupant piped out to
us to stop.</content>
<content>"Walk more slowly, white men," she said, projecting her hideous
shrivelled countenance between the grass curtains, and fixing her
gleaming eyes upon us; "why will ye run to meet the evil that shall
befall you, ye seekers after treasure?"</content>
<content>and she laughed that horrible
laugh which always sent a cold shiver down my back, and for a while
quite took the enthusiasm out of us.</content>
<content>However, on we went, till we saw before us, and between ourselves and
the peak, a vast circular hole with sloping sides, three hundred feet
or more in depth, and quite half a mile round.</content>
<content>"Can't  you guess what this is?"</content>
<content>I said to Sir Henry and Good, who were
staring in astonishment at the awful pit before us.</content>
<content>They shook their heads.</content>
<content>"Then it is clear that you have never seen the diamond diggings at
Kimberley.</content>
<content>You may depend on it that this is Solomon's Diamond Mine.</content>
<content>Look there," I said, pointing to the strata of stiff blue clay which
were yet to be seen among the grass and bushes that clothed the sides
of the pit, "the formation is the same.</content>
<content>I'll be bound that if we went
down there we should find 'pipes' of soapy brecciated rock.</content>
<content>Look, too,"
and I pointed to a series of worn flat slabs of stone that were placed
on a gentle slope below the level of a watercourse which in some past
age had been cut out of the solid rock; "if those are not tables once
used to wash the 'stuff,' I'm a Dutchman."</content>
<content>At the edge of this vast hole, which was none other than the pit marked
on the old Dom's map, the Great Road branched into two and circumvented
it.</content>
<content>In many places, by the way, this surrounding road was built
entirely out of blocks of stone, apparently with the object of
supporting the edges of the pit and preventing falls of reef.</content>
<content>Along
this path we pressed, driven by curiosity to see what were the three
towering objects which we could discern from the hither side of the
great gulf.</content>
<content>As we drew near we perceived that they were Colossi of some
sort or another, and rightly conjectured that before us sat the three
"Silent Ones" that are held in such awe by the Kukuana people.</content>
<content>But it
was not until we were quite close to them that we recognised the full
majesty of these "Silent Ones."</content>
<content>There, upon huge pedestals of dark rock, sculptured with rude emblems
of the Phallic worship, separated from each other by a distance of
forty paces, and looking down the road which crossed some sixty miles
of plain to Loo, were three colossal seated forms - two male and one
female - each measuring about thirty feet from the crown of its head to
the pedestal.</content>
<content>The female form, which was nude, was of great though severe beauty, but
unfortunately the features had been injured by centuries of exposure to
the weather.</content>
<content>Rising from either side of her head were the points of a
crescent.</content>
<content>The two male Colossi, on the contrary, were draped, and
presented a terrifying cast of features, especially the one to our
right, which had the face of a devil.</content>
<content>That to our left was serene in
countenance, but the calm upon it seemed dreadful.</content>
<content>It was the calm of
that inhuman cruelty, Sir Henry remarked, which the ancients attributed
to beings potent for good, who could yet watch the sufferings of
humanity, if not without rejoicing, at least without sorrow.</content>
<content>These
three statues form a most awe-inspiring trinity, as they sit there in
their solitude, and gaze out across the plain for ever.</content>
<content>Contemplating these "Silent Ones," as the Kukuanas call them, an
intense curiosity again seized us to know whose were the hands which
had shaped them, who it was that had dug the pit and made the road.</content>
<content>Whilst I was gazing and wondering, suddenly it occurred to me - being
familiar with the Old Testament - that Solomon went astray after strange
gods, the names of three of whom I remembered - "Ashtoreth, the goddess
of the Zidonians, Chemosh, the god of the Moabites, and Milcom, the god
of the children of Ammon" - and I suggested to my companions that the
figures before us might represent these false and exploded divinities.</content>
<content>"Hum," said Sir Henry, who is a scholar, having taken a high degree in
classics at college, "there may be something in that; Ashtoreth of the
Hebrews was the Astarte of the Phoenicians, who were the great traders
of Solomon's time.</content>
<content>Astarte, who afterwards became the Aphrodite of the
Greeks, was represented with horns like the half-moon, and there on the
brow of the female figure are distinct horns.</content>
<content>Perhaps these Colossi
were designed by some Phoenician official who managed the mines.</content>
<content>Who
can say?"</content>
<content>1 

Before we had finished examining these extraordinary relics of remote

<content>If we were ready to go at once, Gagool had announced her

<content>As it was not later than eleven

<content>Without further ado Gagool plunged into the passage, which was wide

<content>We followed the

<content>"Hullo!</content>
<content>what's that?"</content>

<content>When, so far as we could judge, we had gone some fifty paces, we
perceived that the passage was growing faintly light.</content>
<content>Another minute,
and we were in perhaps the most wonderful place that the eyes of living
man have beheld.</content>
<content>Let the reader picture to himself the hall of the vastest cathedral he
ever stood in, windowless indeed, but dimly lighted from above,
presumably by shafts connected with the outer air and driven in the
roof, which arched away a hundred feet above our heads, and he will get
some idea of the size of the enormous cave in which we found ourselves,
with the difference that this cathedral designed by nature was loftier
and wider than any built by man.</content>
<content>But its stupendous size was the least
of the wonders of the place, for running in rows adown its length were
gigantic pillars of what looked like ice, but were, in reality, huge
stalactites.</content>
<content>It is impossible for me to convey any idea of the
overpowering beauty and grandeur of these pillars of white spar, some
of which were not less than twenty feet in diameter at the base, and
sprang up in lofty and yet delicate beauty sheer to the distant roof.</content>
<content>Others again were in process of formation.</content>
<content>On the rock floor there was
in these cases what looked, Sir Henry said, exactly like a broken
column in an old Grecian temple, whilst high above, depending from the
roof, the point of a huge icicle could be dimly seen.</content>
<content>Even as we gazed we could hear the process going on, for presently with
a tiny splash a drop of water would fall from the far-off icicle on to
the column below.</content>
<content>On some columns the drops only fell once in two or
three minutes, and in these cases it would be an interesting
calculation to discover how long, at that rate of dripping, it would
take to form a pillar, say eighty feet by ten in diameter.</content>
<content>That the
process, in at least one instance, was incalculably slow, the following
example will suffice to show.</content>
<content>Cut on one of these pillars we discovered
the crude likeness of a mummy, by the head of which sat what appeared
to be the figure of an Egyptian god, doubtless the handiwork of some
old-world labourer in the mine.</content>
<content>This work of art was executed at the
natural height at which an idle fellow, be he Phoenician workman or
British cad, is in the habit of trying to immortalise himself at the
expense of nature's masterpieces, namely, about five feet from the
ground.</content>
<content>Yet at the time that we saw it, which must have been nearly
three thousand years after the date of the execution of the carving,
the column was only eight feet high, and was still in process of
formation, which gives a rate of growth of a foot to a thousand years,
or an inch and a fraction to a century.</content>
<content>This we knew because, as we
were standing by it, we heard a drop of water fall.</content>
<content>Sometimes the stalagmites took strange forms, presumably where the
dropping of the water had not always been on the same spot.</content>
<content>Thus, one
huge mass, which must have weighed a hundred tons or so, was in the
shape of a pulpit, beautifully fretted over outside with a design that
looked like lace.</content>
<content>Others resembled strange beasts, and on the sides of
the cave were fanlike ivory tracings, such as the frost leaves upon a
pane.</content>
<content>Out of the vast main aisle there opened here and there smaller caves,
exactly, Sir Henry said, as chapels open out of great cathedrals.</content>
<content>Some
were large, but one or two - and this is a wonderful instance of how
nature carries out her handiwork by the same unvarying laws, utterly
irrespective of size - were tiny.</content>
<content>One little nook, for instance, was no
larger than an unusually big doll's house, and yet it might have been a
model for the whole place, for the water dropped, tiny icicles hung,
and spar columns were forming in just the same way.</content>
<content>We had not, however, enough time to examine this beautiful cavern so
thoroughly as we should have liked to do, since unfortunately, Gagool
seemed to be indifferent as to stalactites, and only anxious to get her
business over.</content>
<content>This annoyed me the more, as I was particularly anxious
to discover, if possible, by what system the light was admitted into
the cave, and whether it was by the hand of man or by that of nature
that this was done; also if the place had been used in any way in
ancient times, as seemed probable.</content>
<content>However, we consoled ourselves with
the idea that we would investigate it thoroughly on our way back, and
followed on at the heels of our uncanny guide.</content>
<content>On she led us, straight to the top of the vast and silent cave, where
we found another doorway, not arched as the first was, but square at
the top, something like the doorways of Egyptian temples.</content>
<content>"Are ye prepared to enter the Place of Death, white men?"</content>
<content>asked Gagool,
evidently with a view to making us feel uncomfortable.</content>
<content>"Lead on, Macduff," said Good solemnly, trying to look as though he was

<content>"This is getting rather ghastly," said Sir Henry, peeping into the dark

<content>"Come on, Quatermain - seniores priores .</content>
<content>We mustn't  keep
the old lady waiting!"</content>
<content>and he politely made way for me to lead the van,
for which inwardly I did not bless him.</content>
<content>Tap, tap, went old Gagool's stick down the passage, as she trotted

<content>"Come, get on, old fellow," said Good, "or we shall lose our fair

<content>Thus adjured, I started down the passage, and after about twenty paces

hollowed, by hand-labour, out of the mountain.</content>
<content>This apartment was not
nearly so well lighted as the vast stalactite ante-cave, and at the
first glance all I could discern was a massive stone table running down
its length, with a colossal white figure at its head, and life-sized
white figures all round it.</content>
<content>Next I discovered a brown thing, seated on
the table in the centre, and in another moment my eyes grew accustomed
to the light, and I saw what all these things were, and was tailing out
of the place as hard as my legs could carry me.</content>
<content>I am not a nervous man in a general way, and very little troubled with
superstitions, of which I have lived to see the folly; but I am free to
own that this sight quite upset me, and had it not been that Sir Henry
caught me by the collar and held me, I do honestly believe that in
another five minutes I should have been outside the stalactite cave,
and that a promise of all the diamonds in Kimberley would not have
induced me to enter it again.</content>
<content>But he held me tight, so I stopped
because I could not help myself.</content>
<content>Next second, however, his eyes
became accustomed to the light, and he let go of me, and began to mop
the perspiration off his forehead.</content>
<content>As for Good, he swore feebly, while
Foulata threw her arms round his neck and shrieked.</content>
<content>Only Gagool chuckled loud and long.</content>
<content>It was a ghastly sight.</content>
<content>There at the end of the long stone table,
holding in his skeleton fingers a great white spear, sat Death

or more in height.</content>
<content>High above his head he held the spear, as though in

<content>asked Good, pointing to the white

<content>said Sir Henry, pointing to the

<content>2  Over the surface of the
corpse there was gathered a thin glassy film, that made its appearance
yet more appalling, for which we were, at the moment, quite unable to
account, till presently we observed that from the roof of the chamber
the water fell steadily, drip!</content>
<content>drop!</content>
<content>drip!</content>
<content>on to the neck of the
corpse, whence it ran down over the entire surface, and finally escaped
into the rock through a tiny hole in the table.</content>
<content>Then I guessed what the
film was - Twala's body was being transformed into a stalactite.</content>
<content>A look at the white forms seated on the stone bench which ran round
that ghastly board confirmed this view.</content>
<content>They were human bodies indeed,
or rather they had been human; now they were stalactites .</content>
<content>This was
the way in which the Kukuana people had from time immemorial preserved
their royal dead.</content>
<content>They petrified them.</content>
<content>What the exact system might be,
if there was any, beyond the placing of them for a long period of years
under the drip, I never discovered, but there they sat, iced over and
preserved for ever by the siliceous fluid.</content>
<content>Anything more awe-inspiring than the spectacle of this long line of
departed royalties  there were twenty-seven of them, the last being
Ignosi's father , wrapped, each of them, in a shroud of ice-like spar,
through which the features could be dimly discovered, and seated round
that inhospitable board, with Death himself for a host, it is
impossible to imagine.</content>
<content>That the practice of thus preserving their kings
must have been an ancient one is evident from the number, which,
allowing for an average reign of fifteen years, supposing that every
king who reigned was placed here - an improbable thing, as some are sure
to have perished in battle far from home - would fix the date of its
commencement at four and a quarter centuries back.</content>
<content>But the colossal Death, who sits at the head of the board, is far older
than that, and, unless I am much mistaken, owes his origin to the same
artist who designed the three Colossi.</content>
<content>He is hewn out of a single
stalactite, and, looked at as a work of art, is most admirably
conceived and executed.</content>
<content>Good, who understands such things, declared
that, so far as he could see, the anatomical design of the skeleton is
perfect down to the smallest bones.</content>
<content>My own idea is, that this terrific object was a freak of fancy on the
part of some old-world sculptor, and that its presence had suggested to
the Kukuanas the idea of placing their royal dead under its awful
presidency.</content>
<content>Or perhaps it was set there to frighten away any marauders
who might have designs upon the treasure chamber beyond.</content>
<content>I cannot say.</content>
<content>All I can do is to describe it as it is, and the reader must form his
own conclusion.</content>
<content>Such, at any rate, was the White Death and such were the White Dead!</content>

 How he sits there and glowers with his head on his knee."</content>
<content>CHAPTER XVII

SOLOMON'S TREASURE CHAMBER

While we were engaged in recovering from our fright, and in examining
the grisly wonders of the Place of Death, Gagool had been differently
occupied.</content>
<content>Somehow or other - for she was marvellously active when she
chose - she had scrambled on to the great table, and made her way to
where our departed friend Twala was placed, under the drip, to see,
suggested Good, how he was "pickling," or for some dark purpose of her
own.</content>
<content>Then, after bending down to kiss his icy lips as though in
affectionate greeting, she hobbled back, stopping now and again to
address the remark, the tenor of which I could not catch, to one or
other of the shrouded forms, just as you or I might welcome an old
acquaintance.</content>
<content>Having gone through this mysterious and horrible
ceremony, she squatted herself down on the table immediately under the
White Death, and began, so far as I could make out, to offer up
prayers.</content>
<content>The spectacle of this wicked creature pouring out
supplications, evil ones no doubt, to the arch enemy of mankind, was so
uncanny that it caused us to hasten our inspection.</content>
<content>"Now, Gagool," said I, in a low voice - somehow one did not dare to
speak above a whisper in that place - "lead us to the chamber."</content>
<content>The old witch promptly scrambled down from the table.</content>
<content>"My lords are not afraid?"</content>
<content>she said, leering up into my face.</content>
<content>"Lead on."</content>
<content>"Good, my lords;" and she hobbled round to the back of the great Death.</content>
<content>"Here is the chamber; let my lords light the lamp, and enter," and she
placed the gourd full of oil upon the floor, and leaned herself against
the side of the cave.</content>
<content>I took out a match, of which we had still a few
in a box, and lit a rush wick, and then looked for the doorway, but
there was nothing before us except the solid rock.</content>
<content>Gagool grinned.</content>
<content>"The
way is there, my lords.</content>
<content>Ha!</content>
<content>ha!</content>
<content>ha! "</content>
<content>"Do not jest with us," I said sternly.</content>
<content>"I jest not, my lords.</content>
<content>See!"</content>
<content>and she pointed at the rock.</content>
<content>As she did so, on holding up the lamp we perceived that a mass of stone
was rising slowly from the floor and vanishing into the rock above,
where doubtless there is a cavity prepared to receive it.</content>
<content>The mass was
of the width of a good-sized door, about ten feet high and not less
than five feet thick.</content>
<content>It must have weighed at least twenty or thirty
tons, and was clearly moved upon some simple balance principle of
counter-weights, probably the same as that by which the opening and
shutting of an ordinary modern window is arranged.</content>
<content>How the principle
was set in motion, of course none of us saw; Gagool was careful to
avoid this; but I have little doubt that there was some very simple
lever, which was moved ever so little by pressure at a secret spot,
thereby throwing additional weight on to the hidden counter-balances,
and causing the monolith to be lifted from the ground.</content>
<content>Very slowly and gently the great stone raised itself, till at last it
had vanished altogether, and a dark hole presented itself to us in the
place which the door had filled.</content>
<content>Our excitement was so intense, as we saw the way to Solomon's treasure
chamber thrown open at last, that I for one began to tremble and shake.</content>
<content>Would it prove a hoax after all, I wondered, or was old Da Silvestra
right?</content>
<content>Were there vast hoards of wealth hidden in that dark place,
hoards which would make us the richest men in the whole world?</content>
<content>We
should know in a minute or two.</content>
<content>"Enter, white men from the Stars," said Gagool, advancing into the
doorway; "but first hear your servant, Gagool the old.</content>
<content>The bright
stones that ye will see were dug out of the pit over which the Silent
Ones are set, and stored here, I know not by whom, for that was done
longer ago than even I remember.</content>
<content>But once has this place been entered
since the time that those who hid the stones departed in haste, leaving
them behind.</content>
<content>The report of the treasure went down indeed among the
people who lived in the country from age to age, but none knew where
the chamber was, nor the secret of the door.</content>
<content>But it happened that a
white man reached this country from over the mountains - perchance he
too came 'from the Stars' - and was well received by the king of that
day.</content>
<content>He it is who sits yonder," and she pointed to the fifth king at
the table of the Dead.</content>
<content>"And it came to pass that he and a woman of the
country who was with him journeyed to this place, and that by chance
the woman learnt the secret of the door - a thousand years might ye
search, but ye should never find that secret.</content>
<content>Then the white man
entered with the woman, and found the stones, and filled with stones
the skin of a small goat, which the woman had with her to hold food.</content>
<content>And as he was going from the chamber he took up one more stone, a large
one, and held it in his hand."</content>
<content>Here she paused.</content>
<content>"Well," I asked, breathless with interest as we all were, "what
happened to Da Silvestra?"</content>
<content>The old hag started at the mention of the name.</content>
<content>"How knowest thou the dead man's name?"</content>
<content>she asked sharply; and then,
without waiting for an answer, went on - 

"None can tell what happened; but it came about that the white man was
frightened, for he flung down the goat-skin, with the stones, and fled
out with only the one stone in his hand, and that the king took, and it
is the stone which thou, Macumazahn, didst take from Twala's brow."</content>
<content>"Have none entered here since?"</content>
<content>I asked, peering again down the dark
passage.</content>
<content>"None, my lords.</content>
<content>Only the secret of the door has been kept, and every
king has opened it, though he has not entered.</content>
<content>There is a saying, that
those who enter there will die within a moon, even as the white man
died in the cave upon the mountain, where ye found him, Macumazahn, and
therefore the kings do not enter.</content>
<content>Ha!</content>
<content>ha!</content>
<content>mine are true words."</content>
<content>Our eyes met as she said it, and I turned sick and cold.</content>
<content>How did the
old hag know all these things?</content>
<content>"Enter, my lords.</content>
<content>If I speak truth, the goat-skin with the stones will
lie upon the floor; and if there is truth as to whether it is death to
enter here, that ye will learn afterwards.</content>
<content>Ha!</content>
<content>ha!</content>
<content>ha! "</content>
<content>and she
hobbled through the doorway, bearing the light with her; but I confess
that once more I hesitated about following.</content>
<content>"Oh, confound it all!"</content>
<content>said Good; "here goes.</content>
<content>I am not going to be
frightened by that old devil;" and followed by Foulata, who, however,
evidently did not at all like the business, for she was shivering with
fear, he plunged into the passage after Gagool - an example which we
quickly followed.</content>
<content>A few yards down the passage, in the narrow way hewn out of the living
rock, Gagool had paused, and was waiting for us.</content>
<content>"See, my lords," she said, holding the light before her, "those who
stored the treasure here fled in haste, and bethought them to guard
against any who should find the secret of the door, but had not the
time," and she pointed to large square blocks of stone, which, to the
height of two courses  about two feet three , had been placed across
the passage with a view to walling it up.</content>
<content>Along the side of the passage
were similar blocks ready for use, and, most curious of all, a heap of
mortar and a couple of trowels, which tools, so far as we had time to
examine them, appeared to be of a similar shape and make to those used
by workmen to this day.</content>
<content>Here Foulata, who had been in a state of great fear and agitation
throughout, said that she felt faint and could go no farther, but would
wait there.</content>
<content>Accordingly we set her down on the unfinished wall, placing
the basket of provisions by her side, and left her to recover.</content>
<content>Following the passage for about fifteen paces farther, we came suddenly
to an elaborately painted wooden door.</content>
<content>It was standing wide open.</content>
<content>Whoever was last there had either not found the time to shut it, or had
forgotten to do so.</content>
<content>Across the threshold of this door lay a skin bag, formed of a
goat-skin, that appeared to be full of pebbles. 

"</content>
<content>Hee!</content>
<content>hee!</content>
<content>white men," sniggered Gagool, as the light from the lamp
fell upon it.</content>
<content>"What did I tell you, that the white man who came here
fled in haste, and dropped the woman's bag - behold it!</content>
<content>Look within also
and ye will find a water-gourd amongst the stones."</content>
<content>Good stooped down and lifted it.</content>
<content>It was heavy and jingled.</content>
<content>"By Jove!</content>
<content>I believe it's full of diamonds," he said, in an awed
whisper; and, indeed, the idea of a small goat-skin full of diamonds is
enough to awe anybody.</content>
<content>"Go on," said Sir Henry impatiently.</content>
<content>"Here, old lady, give me the
lamp," and taking it from Gagool's hand, he stepped through the doorway
and held it high above his head.</content>
<content>We pressed in after him, forgetful for the moment of the bag of
diamonds, and found ourselves in King Solomon's treasure chamber.</content>
<content>At first, all that the somewhat faint light given by the lamp revealed
was a room hewn out of the living rock, and apparently not more than
ten feet square.</content>
<content>Next there came into sight, stored one on the other to
the arch of the roof, a splendid collection of elephant-tusks.</content>
<content>How many
of them there were we did not know, for of course we could not see to
what depth they went back, but there could not have been less than the
ends of four or five hundred tusks of the first quality visible to our
eyes.</content>
<content>There, alone, was enough ivory to make a man wealthy for life.</content>
<content>Perhaps, I thought, it was from this very store that Solomon drew the
raw material for his "great throne of ivory," of which "there was not
the like made in any kingdom."</content>
<content>On the opposite side of the chamber were about a score of wooden boxes,
something like Martini-Henry ammunition boxes, only rather larger, and
painted red.</content>
<content>"There are the diamonds," cried I; "bring the light."</content>
<content>Sir Henry did so, holding it close to the top box, of which the lid,
rendered rotten by time even in that dry place, appeared to have been
smashed in, probably by Da Silvestra himself.</content>
<content>Pushing my hand through
the hole in the lid I drew it out full, not of diamonds, but of gold
pieces, of a shape that none of us had seen before, and with what
looked like Hebrew characters stamped upon them.</content>
<content>"Ah!"</content>
<content>I said, replacing the coin, "we shan't  go back empty-handed,
anyhow.</content>
<content>There must be a couple of thousand pieces in each box, and
there are eighteen boxes.</content>
<content>I suppose this was the money to pay the
workmen and merchants."</content>
<content>"Well," put in Good, "I think that is the lot; I don't  see any
diamonds, unless the old Portuguese put them all into his bag."</content>
<content>"Let my lords look yonder where it is darkest, if they would find the
stones," said Gagool, interpreting our looks.</content>
<content>"There my lords will find
a nook, and three stone chests in the nook, two sealed and one open."</content>
<content>Before translating this to Sir Henry, who carried the light, I could
not resist asking how she knew these things, if no one had entered the
place since the white man, generations ago.</content>
<content>"Ah, Macumazahn, the watcher by night," was the mocking answer, "ye who
dwell in the stars, do ye not know that some live long, and that some
have eyes which can see through rock?</content>
<content>Ha!</content>
<content>ha!</content>
<content>ha! "</content>
<content>"Look in that corner, Curtis," I said, indicating the spot Gagool had
pointed out.</content>
<content>"Hullo, you fellows," he cried, "here's a recess.</content>
<content>Great heavens!</content>
<content>see
here."</content>
<content>We hurried up to where he was standing in a nook, shaped something like
a small bow window.</content>
<content>Against the wall of this recess were placed three
stone chests, each about two feet square.</content>
<content>Two were fitted with stone
lids, the lid of the third rested against the side of the chest, which
was open.

"</content>
<content>See! "</content>
<content>he repeated hoarsely, holding the lamp over the open chest.</content>
<content>We
looked, and for a moment could make nothing out, on account of a
silvery sheen which dazzled us.</content>
<content>When our eyes grew used to it we saw
that the chest was three-parts full of uncut diamonds, most of them of

<content>Stooping, I picked some up.</content>
<content>Yes, there was no doubt
of it, there was the unmistakable soapy feel about them.</content>
<content>I fairly gasped as I dropped them.</content>
<content>"We are the richest men in the whole world," I said.</content>
<content>"Monte Christo was
a fool to us."</content>
<content>"We shall flood the market with diamonds," said Good.</content>
<content>"Got to get them there first," suggested Sir Henry.</content>
<content>We stood still with pale faces and stared at each other, the lantern in
the middle and the glimmering gems below, as though we were
conspirators about to commit a crime, instead of being, as we thought,
the most fortunate men on earth.

"</content>
<content>Hee!</content>
<content>hee!</content>
<content>hee! "</content>
<content>cackled old Gagool behind us, as she flitted about
like a vampire bat.</content>
<content>"There are the bright stones ye love, white men, as
many as ye will; take them, run them through your fingers, eat of

<content>hee!</content>
<content>drink of them, ha!</content>
<content>ha! "</content>
<content>At that moment there was something so ridiculous to my mind at the idea

<content>There we stood
and shrieked with laughter over the gems that were ours, which had been
found for us thousands of years ago by the patient delvers in the
great hole yonder, and stored for us by Solomon's long-dead overseer,
whose name, perchance, was written in the characters stamped on the
faded wax that yet adhered to the lids of the chest.</content>
<content>Solomon never got
them, nor David, or Da Silvestra, nor anybody else.</content>
<content>We had got them:
there before us were millions of pounds' worth of diamonds, and
thousands of pounds' worth of gold and ivory only waiting to be taken
